ZIMBABWE
URBAN SECTOR AND REGIONAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Loan and Project Summary
Borrower:               Zimbabwe
Beneficiariest         Ministry of Local Government, Rural and Urban
Development (MLGRUD);
Ministry of Public Construction and National Housing
(MPCNH);
Zimbabwe Electricity Supply Authority (ZESA);
all the local authorities eligible for General
Development Loan Funds (GDLF) and which meet agreed
criteria;
Central African Building Society (CABS);
Beverly Building Society; and
Founders Building Society.
Amount:                 US$80 million equivalent.
Terms:                 Payable in 20 years, including five years of grace
with variable interest rate.
On-Lending Terms:      Local Authorities: prevailing rates of the GDLF and
the National Housing Fund (NHF), currently
9.75Z p.a. payable in 25 years, including a three-
year grace period; ZESA: prevailing long-term rates
to parastatals, currently 9.752 p.a. payable in
20 years including a five-year grace period; the
building societies (CABS, Beverly, and Founders):
prevailing commercial lending terms including
interest at the prime rate plus a percentage to
cover the foreign exchange risk.
Project Objectives
and Benefits:           The main objectives of the project are to: (i)
provide support to urban areas to st:pply the
additional services required to cope with rapid
population growth; (ii) strengthen the appraisal
capacity of central and local government to
determine the most rational and cost effective
alternative investments in urban areas; (iii) ensure
the continued financial self-sufficiency of the
local authorities; (iv) improve maintenance to
protect the capital assets of the local authorities;
(v) strengthen the human resource capacity of the
local authorities; (vi) maximize the role of private
sector investors in urban housing,; and (vii) assist
with development of regional program focussed on the
smaller towns and rural growth centers.



- ii -
Benefits expected from the project include the
provision of infrastructure to support urban growth
and industrial expansion anticipated in the cities;
provision of housing at affordable standards to the
low and moderate income segments of the population;
increased efficiency of Governmental admiaistration
and more economic utilization of Government
resources in capital development; and replacement of
Governmental resources with private capital to
finance housing development.
Project Description:   The project is structured as a sector loan under
which the Government will undertake to appraise
individual projects in urban areas based on criteria
acceptable to both the Government and the Bank. The
project will: (i) support the provision of primary
urban infrastructure, including water supply,
sewerage, roads and drainage, under the next 5-year
capital development prc3ram, as well as provide for
replenishment and replacement of maintenance-related
vehicles and equipment; (ii) support the provision
of urban housing for about 500,000 people under the
next 5-year capital development program; (iii)
introduce a regional development svrategy, on a
pilot basis, based on support for the preparation of
a Harare Combination Development Plain, a Corridor
Town Development Plan for the Secondary Towns along
the railway between Bulawayo and Mutare, and
District Development Investment Plans for selected
growth areas in Mashonaland East and Manicaland; and
(iv) strengthen the institutional capacity of
Central Government to appraise proposed projects,
and local authorities to perform their planning,
budgeting, financial management, project preparation
and implementation, and urban services maintenance
functions.
Risks:                  rhe principal risks include: possible inability of
Government and the local authorities to meet the
requirements of the Financial Performance Plans
(FPP); and limitations on the capacity of the
building societies to generate tufficient funds.
The risks are mitigated by: Government's commitment
to maintaining the financial independence and
strengthening the operational capacity of the local
authorities; and its interest in shifting housing
finance to the private sector.

ZIMBABWE
URBAN SECTOR AND REGIONAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
I. NATIONAL CONTEXT OF URBAN DEVELOPMENT
A. Macro-Economic Background to Urban Development
1.01     Zimbabwe's overall economic performance has been reasonable since
independence, especially by African standards. Growth of GDP has averaged
about 3-6Z p.a. (with significant annual fluctuations influenced by
agricultural performance), external debt has been effectively managed and
monetary instability has been avoided.  However, both the Government of
Zimbabwe (GOZ) &nd the private sector now have serious concerns over the
weakness of future growth prospects and the:e are fears that the economy
will not be able to provide adequate incomes, employment, and basic
services to a population growing at ovtr 32 p.a.  These concerns are well-
founded. Analysis indicates that sustainable growth will require a major
r4se in the investment rate, especially in the productive sectors, in order
to support a process of gradual restructuring in manufacturing and
diversification in agriculture. A combination of significant macroeconomic
adjustments and structural reforms w'll be necessary to encourage a rise in
the investment rate, assure a sustainable external payments position and
provide a supportive climate for the private sector.
1.02     Macroeconomic restructuring and growth are relevant to the design
of urban policy in three key areas: in the economy's medium and long-term
requirements for urban infrastructure; in the relationship to budgetary
management; and in the adaptation of local authorities to required shifts
in the pattern of growth to meet the country's employment problem.
1.03     First, Zimbabwe is under-urbanized relative to its structure of
production (unlike Zambia, for instance). The level of urbanization is
estimated at 272, about average for Africa, yet manufacturing already
accounts for a quarter of total production, more than that of any other
Sub-Saharan country, barring South Africa. The sector is reasonably
efficient and its relative share is expected to continue to grow within an
effective growth strategy. Past under-urbanization is a consequence of the
racially based location and labor policies of pre-independence governments
and the 1980s have already witnessed an acceleration of urbanization. This
has so far not led to severe strains on urban infrastructure for either the
productive sector or households, largely because there has been little or
no expansion in industrial capacity since independence; the combination of
densification within established households and government-sponsored urban
settlement schemes was able to deal with the bulk of new migrants. The
situation is likely to be quite different over the next decade, however,
especially if the Government is successful in implementing a growth-
oriented adjustment program. A substantial expansion of basic urban
infrastructure will be necessary to support urban-based economic
development and accommodate urban population growth.



1.04     Second, the structural reforms will have an important bearing on
the futare availability of financial resources for urban infrastructure and
housing. The critical macroeconomic adjustment required is a reduction in
the public sector deficit, that has been running at around 9 - 1O of GDP.
Closely related to such adjustment is a reorientation of the financial
system toward the channeling of private savings into private investment.
Zimbabwe has a highly developed financial system, particularly with respect
to the non-monetary sector, which has been very effective both in capturing
private savings and in providing banking services to business. However,
the dominant flow of funds within the financial system has been from
private deposits to the purchase of government bonds. This has allowed
Government to finance the deficit in a non-inflationary manner (in the
short run), but has led to a severe diversion of resources away from
productive inv4.stment. Since the core of the deficit problem lies in the
level of current spending (includ'ng subsidies to parastatals) this low
level of private investment has not been offset by higher public
investment. Effective adjustment, dependent on improved financial
management, will require two changes: first, a reduction in the amount of
financing undertaken through the budget, which will have to be largely
effected through reduced current spending; and second, the encouragement of
the financial system to develop its capabilities for investment finance,
both for established businesses and households and for new smaller-scale
economic activities and households. There is a particular opportunity for
the development of a capability in the building societies for low-cost
housing finance. The achievement of these reforms in public and private
finance and improvements in macroeconomic performance will also depend to a
significant extent on the ability of local governments to maintain a sound
financial position, since any deterioration in the financial strength of
local authorities will lead to greater demands on the Central Government
budget (and/or a reduction of service levels).
1.05     Third, while growth of the formal sector, especially
manufacturing, will be necessary for overall economic development and
macroeconomic balance, it will only make a limited contribution to
Zimbabwe's employment problem: indicative projections suggest that of the
order of 75Z of the expansion of the labor force will be absorbed outside
formal employment in the next decade. A major portion of this component of
the labor force will need to find work in the peri-urban parts if the large
towns and in smaller growth centers. This pressure of job seekers will
require measures to develop the informal sector and the provision of an
urban environment and policies that are supportive. Urban policy can
facilitate this development in three ways: through encouraging rapid growth
in low-cost housing investment that depends on small contractors (direct
and indirect employment effects based on simpl.e techniques could be
substantial); through improving the basic infrastructure in poorly serviced
settlements with good growth potential; and through providing the policy
and institutienal framework conducive to the growth of small informal
businesses, in both the productive and service sectors.



- 3 -
B. Urban Development and the Supply of Infrastructure
Sectoral Distribution of Economic Activity
1.06     Between the two main productive sectors of agriculture and
industry, agriculture (including forestry), the main economic activity of
rural areas, produced only about 142 of the 1988 GDP. This compares with
industry which contributed Z$ 1.5 billion or over 362 out of the total Z$
4.1 billior. GDP produced in that year, of which nearly Z$ :.0 billion or
252 of total GDP was contributed by urban-based manufactu-ing. The service
sector comprised the rest, of which most was also urban-based. The
industrial sector, while stagnant during the 1983-88 period, is projected,
as noted above, as a major source of growth in the economy under any
restructuring program. Consequently, with most manufacturing located in
the cities, the qualit,, reliability, and efficiency of urban
infrastructure services will play an important role in supporting a
national development strategy focussed on the productive sectors.
Demographic Trends
1.07     While the population of Zimbabwe, estimated at 8.7 million, is
growing at the relatively rapid rate of 3.2Z p.a., urban population is
increasing much faster at an annual rate of about 62 (and perhaps even
higher). About 2.3 million people currently live in towns of 15,000 people
or more. Large numbers of people are moving from the rural areas, where
densities in the communal lands, combined with attempts to increase
agricultural productivity, and high and underemployment propel this shift.1
Consequently, over half a million people are being added to the urban
population each year (see Table I.1). This trend has been strongly in
evidence since independence and is expected to continue at similar or
higher rates over the next decade. By the year 2000, Zimbabwe will
therefore have undergone a demographic transformation whereby the cities
which had accommodated 172 of the population at independence, will be
grappl'ng with the provision of services and infrastructure for about 50Z
of the country's population. The development of an urban infrastructure
investment strategy that balances the pressures of future demand with
available resources for urban services, and still maintains a capacity to
deliver, operate, and finance such services, has emerged as an important
element of Government policy.
1/ The Zimbabwe Urban Sector Review (ZIM-4171, 1985) estimated that even
with a relatively successful resettlement program and good performance in
overall agricultural output, given the high rate of population growth, a
rate of increase of the urban population of about 102 p.a. was required
for any improvements in rural incomes to occur.

Distribution of Towns
1.08     The distribution of cities and towns around the country was
profoundly influenced by the Zimbabwe National Railway; nearly all
commercial and ina&istrial centers were established along the rail lines.
Fortunately, the railway network is extensive, and has resulted in a
widespread distribution of service, marketing, processing, and
manufacturing towns. This represents a valuable asset as these towns,
relatively equitably located in relation to the prime agricultural and more
densely populated rural hinterlands, have experienced a proportionate share
of the population growth in recent years, and have continued to fulfill
important economic roles in the development of their regions. The network



of towns is generally well placed to serve the Communal Areas, althouglk
linkages from the Communal Lands to the towns require improvements; and in
some instances where these distances are too great, and where rural
economic activities have accelerated, new growth centers have emerged (e.g.
Mrewa, GokwO).
1.09     Another important advantage to the distribution of urban areas in
Zimbabwe has been the disaggregated demand pressures for such urban
services as water supply and sewerage treatment. A larger number of
smaller centers has allowed for local, less bulky investments in primary
infrastructure in urban areas, as well as more equitable distribution of
resources regionally. In addition, this has resulted in a more effective
spreading of responsibilities for generating local revenues to pay for
these infrastructure costs. Successful Government policy requires that the
secondary towns retain their vigorous economic role, and depends on their
maintaining a capacity to provide infrastructure services competitive with
the large cities, and on their ability to sustain their financial
independence in the process.
Demand for Housing-Related Infrastructure
1.10     The housing market in Zimbabwe prior to independence could be
characterized as comprising two essentially separate delivery systems. One
was private sector based and served middle and upper income households.
The other was a public sector telivery program designed for low-Income
households that was implemented by local authorities and financed by them
with borrowings from Central Government. Following independence,
requirements for low-income housing, still fully dependent on public sector
napital development funds, accelerated sharply, at the same time that
Government turned its attention to the long neglected development needs of
the Communal Areas.
1.11     Almost all new urban population growth comprises low and moderate
income households; that is households dependent on Government financed
housing development. It is estimated that to keep pace with infrastructure
requirements for housing in the urban areas, an average of approximately
15,000 - 30,000 new res-dential sites would have to be serviced annually
over the next decade, and this would have to be doubled to eliminate the
existing backlog. It is unlikely that this level of supply can be
achieved, particularly given the high degree of dependence of the housing
infrastructure program on public sector resources. A strategy is therefore
required that efficiently balances housing-related tertiary infrastructure
investments with city-wide primary infrastructure needs while concurrently
ensuring adequate capacity in the systerm to meet the demands of the other
sectors, particularly those of industry. A key consideration will be the
potential role of the private sector as an alternative source of long-term
financing for residential infrastructure.
Current Status and Future Requirements of Urban Infrastructure
1.12     The success of the urban local authority system in Zimbabwe is
most evident in the effective capacity demonstrated by the cities and towns
in supplying, operating, and maintaining infrastructure services over the
years. The absorption of the large shifts in population to the urban



-6-
areas, and the steady performance of the industrial sector, which has been
able to rely on dependable and efficient urban services, is attributable to
the independence and self-sufficiency of the town councils. As a result,
over 902 of the urbans population enjoy satisfactory access to water supply,
sanitation, road networks and public transportation facilities, drainage,
and solid waste management. The other users of the facilities, including
in particular, the industrial sector, have been able to operate and plan
for growth on the ready availability of additional infrastructure capacity
when required.
1.13     In part, the performance of the local authorities can be traced to
a resource-rich period for the urban sector, prior to independence, when
public investments favored the cities at the expense of the Communal Areas.
As a result, substantial depth in bulk infrastructure capacity and in
standards and levels of supply were established. With the post-
independence period of emphasis on redressing imbalances in the rural
areas, investment in real terms in urban infrastructure has been curtailed.
The pattern of this investment has also been modified, with a greater share
of resources being spent on capital works rather than maintenance-related
plant and equipment in order to cope with the inflow of new urban migrants.
The depth of the existing system and the thoughtful allocation of the
limited finances for new infrastructure has enabled the system to sustain a
reliable delivery of urban services, but this is increasingly facing
capacity stress. Estimates of need resulting from accumulated deferments
and the rapid growth of the cities indicate that ideally about Z$ 0.5
billion would be needed over the next five years. well in excess of
affordable levels of investment.
1.14     The successful performance of urban government in Zimbabwe in
supplying infrastructure is therefore at a crossroads. It is estimated
that, despite strong Government commitment to maintaining suitable levels
of investment in the urban areas, cnly about 25-302 of the required
resources will be available from the public sector. At risk are (i) an
erosion of the depth in infrastructure capacity such that intractable
service deficiencies emerge. Of particular concern would be the inability
to adequately respond to any structural adjustment initiatives requiring
industrial capacity expansion that depends, in turn, on infrastructure
services; (i.i) depletion of maintenance capabilities resulting in
irremediable deterioration of major capital assets in the urban areas;2
and (iii) the rapid growth in the urban population outstripping the supply
of infrastructure and the creation of informal, squatter settlements which
has, to date, been avoided.
C. Management of the Budget and Local Authority Financial Performance
Background to Local Authority Responsibilities
1.15     The Local Government system in the urban areas is a de facto
unitary system with only one authority responsible for the affairs of each
2/ While deterioration of the assets themselves has not occurred on any
significant scale in most of the urban areas, the maintenance vehicles and
equipment are seriously depleted and their replacement is urgently
required to avoid rapid deterioration of existing infrastructure.



-7-.
area. The primary concern of the urban councils is the provision of water
supplies, sewerage systems, roads and transport-related infrastructure
services, trunk road access (non-gazetted), housing-related infrastructure
and low-cost housing, street lighting, as vell as health, educational, and
welfare services. Central Government, through the Ministries of Local
Government, Rural and Urban Development (MLGRUD) and Public Construction
and National Housing (MPCNH), monitors the performance of the Councils and
assists them with capital development funds, but on a loan basis only.
Each authority is autonomous in directing its own affairs, and virtually
all current expenditures are the responsibility of the local authorities,
which they finance out of local revenues generated from utility and service
tariffs, fees, and property taxes in the form of rates. The only
subsidized transfers comprise small grants from the line ministries for
health and education purposes, largely to offset the relatively recent
devolution of tertiary health and primary education facilities and
operations to the local authorities.
Local Authority Recurrent Expenditure Performance
1.16     The financial independence of the local authorities, whether taken
alone or when compared with the public sector in general (particularly the
parastatals), stands out as a singularly valuable asset in overall
Government budgetary operations.  Local authority current expenditures
comprise a substantial proportion of total public sector current
expenditures, equivalent to about 112. Moreover, despite the tremendous
population growth pressures on the local authorities, the rate of increase
of their debt has been a little over half that of Central Government;
whereas gross public sector debt rose over 3302 between 1980 and 1987,
local authority debt increased by a little less than 2002 for the same
period. Even more significantly, whereas the Central Government deficit
increased from a little more than Z$ 0.3 billion in 1980 to over Z$ 1.0
billion in 1987, (a 300Z increase), the local authorities, when reviewed
collectively, have continued to generate a surplus. This is attributable
to relatively solid financial management discipline, and to their capacity
and willingness to raise and collect local revenues to match their
expenditures. Given their relatively large share of total public sector
current expenditures, the financial health of the urban councils is of
particular interest to Government. Any erosion of this performance towards
deficit financing would introduce subsidies into the urban sector at a time
when the Government's effort to reduce the public sector deficit is a
priority policy objective.
1.17     It is this prospect which is of concern to Government, as the
sound overall financial condition of the urban areas masks the uneven
situation that exists between individual authorities. Here, trends in
local authority performance with current expenditure control are at issue.
Increases in expenditure in several instances appear to have followed a
somewhat similar growth pattern to those in Central Government, which grew
from a little over Z$ 1.0 billion in 1980 to over Z$ 3.5 billion in 1987, a
nearly fourfold increase. When Central Government subsidies to parastatals
are included, this increases by a further 152, and it is the concern for
managing public expenditure transfers that is of particular interest when
examining trends in local authority finances. While not yet critical, the
signs of financial deterioration occurring in some of the councils indicate



- a - 8
that steps to define a recovery strategy, improve financial management,
introduce effective financial performance planning that includes investment
and revenue programming and monitoring procedures, and undertake some
remedial accounting measures, be treated as a priority of urban policy.
Capital Development Investments by Local Authorities
1.18     Local authorities depend primarily on resource transfers from
Central Government for their capital development investment programs.
Virtually all such transfers are in the form of loans, and the local
authorities depend on their own revenue generating sources to service these
debts. The towns also draw on reserves for investment purposes, and the
councils with city status (Harare, Bulawayo, Gweru, and Mutare) can issue
bonds, subject to MLGRUD and Ministry of Finance, Economic Planning and
Development (MFEPD) approval. Local authority capital expenditures have
increased at a slower average rate than inflation between 1980 and 1988,
though quite significant real incres-es took place in the early post-
independence years.  The competitio.. Pr Government capital development
funds and the consequent rcduction of investment in real terms in urban
infrastructure during recent years presents one of the most urgent areas
for new urban policy initiatives, and it is in this area where significant
additional investment from the private sector is in prospect. A strategy
to gradually replace long term Government financing of residential
infrastructure and housing construction with private sector funds is under
development. If this can occur on a sufficiently large scale, then the
housing program can be expanded, while at the same time allowing resources
to be reallocated to primary infrastructure such that real growth in
investment can take place in both urban infrastructure and housirg.
D. Regional Distribution of Urban Development and Employment
1.19     As a result of the population pressures on the Communal Lands,
Zimbabwe towns will continue to grow at a rapid rate, creating heavy
demands for employment. Therefore, Central Government and the local
authorities face a pressing problem of designing policies and implementing
programs to encourage development of commerce and industry in those towns.
It is probable however, that such programs will not keep pace with
migration to the urban areas, and that the high growth of the labor force
in the cities is likely to cause both rising unemployment and continued low
incomes for many, resulting in affordability problems relative to the cost
of supplying urban infrastructure services. The tendency of this shift in
population to focus on the primary cities places a significant burden on
them; the proportionately higher number of low income households there
exacerbates problems of affordability of services. Also, the
disproportionately higher population growth rate in the large cities
frequently results in bulky primary infrastructure investment requirements
that involve substantially higher unit costs. Moreover, this uneven spread
of urban migration with its attendant investment demands for the main
centers can result in the neglect of the secondary towns and smaller
regional centers. Such neglect could seriously erode the developmental
benefits gererated by the existing network of widely distributed towns with
their strong economic linkages to their rural hinterlands, and fail to
exploit the potential for similar economic relationships that could be
fostered in the Communal Areas.



- 9 -
1.20     Special attention should therefore be given to introducing
development strategies and investment plans for both the secondary town
network and the high potential districts with their small but rapidly
growing urban centers. In particular, the Gweru-Mutare railway corridor
(via Harare) represents a system of towns and related high-output mining or
agricultural regions that would benefit from a well articulated development
program. Similarly, a suitable system of district development planning and
investment programming should be undertaken for key districts with
demonstrated economic potential, focussed on their urban growth centers;
such an effort would initiate a process for supporting economic growth in
those rural areas where nearly 60Z of the national population live.
However, unemployment in the urban areas will continue to increase to high
levels even with a successful regional development strategy and a gradual
shift to a more evenly distributed pattern of urban greoth in which
employment opportunities are maximized. Therefore, tha development
strategies proposed for the towns and district centers will require
a major component aim4'd at the strengthening of the informal sector.
E. Government Policy in the Urban Sector
1.21     The First Five-Year National Development Plan (1986 to 1990)
arnticipates a significant shift in the distribution of population towards
the urban areas. on a scale described in para. 1.07. The magnitude of the
problem and its impact on urban investment requirements in essential
services, and on the urgency of maintaining infrastructure capacity to
support employment generation initiatives in the productive sectors, are
recognized in the Plan. Priority is also given, under the Plan to
stabilizing regional shifts in population in the secondary towns to ease
the concentration in demand for urban services in the primary cities, and
to strengthen the economic role of the small centers in regions of good
growth potential. Government has therefore introduced a number of policies
to address the constraints facing the urban sector, and to promote the role
played by urban areas in complementing economic growth support programs.
Key elements of Government's urban strategy include:
(a) Making the most efficient use of the scarce resources
available. This requires strengthening the capacities of the
line ministries with urban-related responsibilities, and the
local authorities, to effectively evaluate the technical,
financial and economic consequences of all proposed
investments. This would support: (i) improvements in the
efficient selection and scheduling of competing urban
irvestments, and in the identification of the most cost-
effective capital development proposals; (ii) maximizing the
use of existing system capacities, allowing for postponement
of bulkier investments by better balancing existing
facilities; (iii) planning of major capital works to avoid
lumpy funding demands; (iv) establishment of a suitable
balance in the allocation of capital development resources
between maintenance and expansion; and (v) greater
opportunities for the secondary towns and smaller centers to
compete for scarce capital development resources;



-10 -
(b) The involvement of private sector intermediation (the
building societies) in financing low income housing in order
to substantially expand the funds available for housing and
thus significantly increase low cost housing infrastructure
provision and house construction.   This participation of the
building societies would also initiate a process of
substituting Government-sourced transfers for capital
development financing with funds from the private sector;
(c) The maintenance of the financial independence and viability
of local authorities in order to assure the continuity of
these entities as the suppliers of urban services, and to
avoid any shift in dependence towards Central Government
current expenditure transfers to urban sector, as well as to
minimize the reliance on GOZ financing of capital works.
Government strategy is to introduce effective manpower
development programs in order to strengthen institutional
capacity in all facets of local authority operations, but
with particular emphasis on financial management. In
addition, GOZ is proposing the introduction of a new
management tool, the Financial Performance Plan (FPP), which
would: (i) enable continuous mon4toring of the financial
circumstances of each local authority; (ii) bring into
operation a means of future planning and programming; and
(iii) spell out both local and central government agreements
on actions to be taken and performance standards to be met in
order for the local authority to qualify for access to
investment loans;
(d) Promoting the secondary cities and towns, and the smaller
regional growth centers, in competition with the main
centers, primarily through the strengthened appraisal
procedures described in (a) above. It is expected that
support for preparing more complete feasibility studies for
proposed infrastructure and housing developments, as part of
the annual investment review cycle, will enable, submissions
by the smaller centers to more effectively compete for
consideration. In addition, Government is planning to
initiate regional development programs that more cle%rly
articulate investment strategies for the secondary centers
and focus on the employment generation opportunities offered
under these strategies.
F. Donors' Role in the Urban Sector
1.22     The donors involved in the urban sector in Zimbabwe have mainly
comprised the United States Agency for International Development (USAID),
the British Overseas Development Agency (ODA), the Swedish International
Development Agency (SIDA), the Commonwealth Development Corporation (CDC)
and the Bank. USAID provided assistance in the development of a major
low-income housing program in a number of cities and towns in Zimbabwe.
The program, which started shortly after independence and is nearing
completion, included technical assistance and training in the housing
subsector. ODA has provided primarily technical assistance to MLGRUD to
undertake training of local authority staff in urban areas in Zimbabwe.



- 11 -
SIDA has also focussed on training-related technical assistance for local
authorities, provided through MLGRUD. While SIDA has focussed on the
district councils, the overlap with the smaller urban areas has been
considerable, particularly with the move by Government towards the
amalgamation of rural and district councils. In addition, SIDA has
provided substantial support in examining the requirements for effectuating
the amalgamation. CDC participated with the Bank in cofinancing the Bank-
assisted Urban Development Project (ZIM-2445), Urban I, under which the
private sector, through the building societies, was introduced to low-
income housing mortgage lending. CDC played a key role in facilitating
building society intermediation in public housing which has proven to be a
major opportunity for expansion of Government's low cost housing program.
1.23     The Bank's involvement in the sector began in 1980, leading to the
preparation of the Zimbabwe Urban Sector Report, which served as the basis
for Urban I. The primary objective of the First Urban Project, which is
based in four cities (Harare, Bulawayo, Mutare, and Masvingo), was the
initiation of a large scale housing program in %hich Government investments
in housing could be complemented with long term private sector financing in
order to significantly increase the supply of housing. The project was
designed to ensure affordability to low-income urban residents through the
provision of least-cost housing solutions, and replicability through
continued sound cost recovery procedures. The project, though not
explicitly designated as such, was intended to serve as a pilot operation
which, if successful, could be further expanded. Urban I also includes
technical assistance to support a local authority training program, as well
as providing off-site infrastructure and community facilities linked to the
requirements of the housing financed under the project.
Rationale for Bank Involvement
1.24     While Urban I is not yet complete, it is evident from reviews of
progress made to date that the project is being effectively implemented,
and has been instrumental in assisting Government in laying the foundation
for a housing program with a broader resource base. The housing and
infrastructure services have been built as designed, at cost, and in
advance of the original schedule, and fully satisfactory cost recovery
performance has been maintained. The proposed project therefore builds on
the success of the first, expanding the role of private sector financial
intermediation for housing from an essentially pilot operation, to a
national sector-wide program. In addition, the project would support
Government initiatives in: strengthening local authority institutional
capacity for operating and maintaining urban services; and assuring
continued local authority financial autonomy by providing financial
management training, improved project appraisal procedures, and new
mechanisms for linking local authority expenditures to financial
performance. The project also supports Government development policy
(reflected in the Bank's Country Assistance Strategy) of reducing the need
for Central Government transfers, by maintaining the financial viability of
the local authorities in order to avoid the introduction of subsidies into
the urban sector, and ensuring a capacity to support 'urban growth and
industrial development through sector-wide investment in capital works and
maintenance-related requirements for primary infrastructure.



-12-
II. THE PROJECT
A. Objectives
2.01     The principal objectives of the project are to:
(a) provide support to urban centers in supplying additional
urban services and housing that will be required as a result
of the projected doubling of the urban population over the
next 7 to 10 years;
(b) strengthen the capacity of central and local Government
institutions to determine the most cost-effective investments
in the urban sector and establish the most efficient
scheduling for these investments;
(c) ensure the continued financial self-sufficiency of the urban
local authorities, including the maintenance of appropriate
cost recovery and revenue generation capabilities;
(d) protect the capital assets of urban local authorities through
improved maintenance-related investments;
(e) strengthen the human resource capacity of local authorities
to enable them to efficiently deliver, operate and maintain
urban services on a sustained basis;
(f) maximize the role of non-Governmental, private sector,
investors in housing, particularly building societies, as a
means of relieving the financial burden on Government;
(g) assist with the design and implementation of regional
development programs focussed on the secondary cities, small
towns and rural centers as alternatives to the major cities,
emphasizing in particular the promotion of employment
creation irn the urban areas to absorb the growing labor
force.
B. Project Scope
Composition
2.02     The project comprises the following principal components:
(a) Primary Urban Infrastructure providing support for the next
five-year capital investment program for urban areas covering
primary infrastructure (including maintenance requirements).
Bank assistance would be in the form of a sub-sector loan to
the Government with funds onlent through the General
Development Loan Fund (GDLF) for specific subprojects;
(b) Housing and Related Serviced Residential Land providing
support for the next five-year capital investment plan for
urban areas covering housing and housing-related serviced
residential land. Bank assistance would be in the form of a



- 13 -
sub-sector loan to the Government for residential
infrastructure development, with funds onlent through the
National Housing Fund (NHF) for specific subprojects. House
construction costs and the purchase of the serviced plots
would be financed bv long-term mortgages to be provided by
the building societies directly to the individual
beneficiaries;
(c) Regional Development Program introducing a regional
development strategy under which two pilot programs would be
implemented. The first would provide technical assistance to
prepare a strategic investment framework for the development
of an urban services infrastructure in the secondary towns
along the Bulawayo/Harare/Mutare railway corridor, to be
financed under the sub-sector loans mentioned above.
Emphasis would be placed on generating employment
opportunities and promoting small-enterprise development.
The second would assist with promoting the growth of selected
small towns and rural centers in two provinces, Mashonaland
East and Manicaland. This would include technical assistance
to support, on a pilot basis, preparation of selected
district development investment plans in infrastructure and
services related to employment support measures as a means of
expanding and diversifying non-farm activities.
(d) Strengthening Institutional Capacity through a manpower
development program that would include technical assistance
and training to Central Government line ministries and local
authorities. The program would address investment
programming and project appraisal capacities, as well as
local authority financial management, and urban service
operation and maintenance requirements;
Selection of Proiect Towns
2.03     All towns with borrowing powers under the GDLF can participate in
the project, subject to their meeting certain eligibility criteria which
were agreed on during appraisal and confirmed at negotiations, and are
described in Annex 1. Currently, 50 local autherities (see Arnex 2 for a
list), of which 16 are urban councils and 34 are towns in rursl councils,
have access to GDLF loan funds. Any additional towns which may become
eligible for GDLF resources during project execution would be able to
participate under the project subject to satisfying the criteria in Annex
1. During appraisal the financial condition and investment needs of the
following 21 towns were determined to have satisfied the eligibility
criteria.
Urban Councils
City Councils: Harare, Bulawayo, Gweru, Mutare
Municipal Councils: Kadoma, Kwekwe, Chegutu, Hasvingo,
Chinoyi, Marondera, Redcl'ff
Town Councils: Shurugwi, Victoria Falls, Kariba, Hwange,
Chitungwiza



-.14-
Rural Councils
Town Boards: Bindura, Rusape, Zvishavane, Owanda, Nyanga
All towns eligible to participate under the project would be required to
prepare a FPP, which would be subject to approval by MLGRUD, and should be
satisfactory to the Bank (see paras. 2.25, 3.05, and 4.12).
2.04     The towns in the project represent a population (1982 census) of
about 1.96 million. Therefore, the project will directly affect the
delivery of infrastructure, housing and related services of towns
containing virtually all of the urban population in Zimbabwe. Annex 3
contains projections of population, households and housing demand for the
21 towns that were reviewed in detail during project preparation/appraisal.
C. Detailed Project Description
Sector Approach
2.05     The financing of the two major sub-components of the project,
primary urban infrastructure and urban houGing (including related community
facilities and electrical reticulation), which together comprise about 86Z
of total project base costs (excluding housing finance), has been
structured as two sub-sector loans under which the Government will appraise
and provide financing for individual projects proposed by local authorities
for investments in urban infrastructure and housing respectively. The sub-
sector loans would finance the foreign currency requirements of the public
sector investment program (PSIP) of GOZ. All capital development and
maintenance investments in primary and housing-related infrastructure in
the eligible towns would qualify under the sub-sector loans. Appraisal
procedures and criteria for evaluating proposed investments, and their
linkage to the PSIP, are described in paras. 3.04 to 3.07.
Primary Urban Infrastructure
2.06     Infrastructure Works.  During project preparation, a detailed
technical assessment and estimate of costs of infrastructure investment
needs was undertaken for each of the 21 local authorities, including
maintenance-related vehicle and equipment requirements for urban services,
based on existing and projected demand and on existing levels of provision
(summarized in Annex 4). The assessment was linked to the housing program
as well as to related demands for industrial, commercial, public
administration and community/health services resulting from urban growth.1
1/ Samples of these analyses are given in Annexes 5, 6, and 7. Similar
analyses for the remaining 18 local authorities are available as part of
the project implementation file, and are contained in a supplementary
document available to GOZ to assist with project execution.



- 15 -
2.07     The capital investment requirements represented by the needs
analysis, which were limited to well justified deficiencies only, comprised
a significantly greater share of the GOZ capital development program than
under past PSIP allocations. However, it was concluded during appraisal
that ceilings reflecting past expenditure levels could be adopted, while
still ensuring satisfactory standard of essential infrpitructure
provision, subject to the introduction of modifications in (i) resources
allocated for maintenance requirement; and (ii) past policies of allowing
allocations for urban services to erode in real terms. The level of
investment in capital development requirements for the first year of the
sector program consequently was set at Z$ 25.0 million, which was estimated
to be the average achieved annually over the preceding five years;
thereafter it would be maintained at that level in real terms for the
remainder of the sector loan period. Funding to be earmarked for
investment in maintenance-related vehicles, plant and equipment, would be
included as an additional amount to offset the emphasis that has been
placed in recent years on capital works and to redress the serious
deterioration in the maintenance capacities of local authorities. The
proposed level of investment was determined to be appropriate to address
critical backlog requirements for infrastructure development and
maintenance, as well as accommodate some expansion in demand, while taking
account of the public expenditure constraints anticipated by GOZ and the
Bank.
2.08     Water supply works (Z$ 68 million) will include bulk water
augmentation, treatment, pumping, storage, trunk mains and reticulation.
With regard to sewerage (Z$ 35 million), the improvements are trunk mains,
reticulation, pumping and treatment facilities. Road and drainage works
(ZS 23 million) will include all non-gazetted roads. The first two-yea;
program to be financed under the sector loan will be derived from the
schedule of inv;estments in infrastructure that was reviewed in detail
during appraisal and was determined to be technically feasible. The
selection from among the proposed investments will be determined during the
PSIP process according to resource and affordability criteria mentioned
above (para. 2.07). The services required to design the various primary
infrastructure facilities and to supervise their construction are included
under this component, whether performed by the local authority staff,
consultants or the Urban Development Corporation (UDCORP) engaged by these
authorities.
2.09     Urban Services and Infrastructure Maintenance.  This component
(Z$ 81 million) of the project, which comprises part of the primary
infrastructure sub-sector loan, provides for plant, equipment and vehicles
required by local authorities to maintain service levels and protect past
investments in infrastructure. Although emphasis is placed on the supply
of spare parts, substantial replacement and/or augmentation of plant,
vehicles and equipment is required, as determined during appraisal, because
of the now urgent need to protect capital assets where the potential for
rapid deterioration is posing a threat of premature loss of the assets.
Schedules of plant, vehicles and equipment requirements have been prepared
for each town taking account of the age and condition of existing assets,
and these are listed in Annex 8.



- 16-
Housing and Related Serviced Residential Land
2.10     Serviced Residential Land.  A detailed technical assessment of low
and moderate income housing needs in each of the towns included under the
project was undertaken during project preparation. The assessment was
based on the existing housing backlog as measured by updated waiting lists
maintained by the local authorities, and on estimates of growth in demand
(see Annex 3). During appraisal these were modified to reflect resources
available within the framework of projected public sector investment
constraints. The amount of investment consequently agreed on was linked to
recent patterns of public resource allocatians to housing. In the first
year of the project, an allocation of Z$ 45.0 million was agreed, which
matched the average annual budgetary commitment under the PSIP for housing
over the past five years. This level of investment would then be
maintained in real terms. It was also agreed that not less than 60' of
this annual allocation would be directed to the project, of which the
sector loan would finance the foreign currency requirements. The proposed
level of investment was estimated to be sufftcient to keep pace with growth
in demand. During project preparation a number of sites for housing were
identified in several of the towns participating under the project, and
layout design work was completed. During the FY89/90 PSIP and appraisal
process, GOZ would make a selection from among these alternative projects
in determining the composition of the investments in housing to be financed
in the first year of the subsector loan. Electricity reticulation
requirements to provide for residential connections, are included under the
project (Z$ 39 million).
2.11     The project will include the provision of serviced residential
plots for low, moderate and lower-middle income households in all the
eligible cities. In general, the urban councils have sufficient land for
expansion of housing; a few councils will have to expand their town areas
by acquiring adjacent farm land on the basis of the well-established
practice of negotiated purchase from willing sellers. The minimum size
plot is 300 m2, but Government is considering reducing this to 200m2. Each
plot will have full road access, a connection to a mid-block sewer, a water
connection and an optional electricity connection. Infrastructure
standards have been adopted to minimize costs and maximize efficiencies.
Based on current occupancy rates, it is estimated that the plots to be
provided will accommodate over 500,000 people including both occupants and
renters or lodgers. Servicing of land for local commercial and industrial
requirements and community facilities is also included; taken together,
these land uses represent about 202 of the gross area of each project site
while residential land will comprise about 502 of the gross area.
2.12     The community facilities to be provided (Z$ 29 million) comprise
primary schools, clinics, market areas and community centers. The level of
provision under the project, in terms of facilities-to-service population
ratios, will be in line with current standards actually implemented in the
cities. Only community facilities in new housing areas being developed
under the project would qualify for funding under the sector loan. The
community facilities proposed to be developed under the project, including
the standards and design of the facilities, for each such area would be
forwarded to the Bank for comments prior to commencement of the tendering
process for construction of any of the facilities in the area being
developed. This was confirmed during negotiations. The designs of



- 17-
facilities are based on revised minimum standards of construction as
implemented under the First Urban Development Project and will be reviewed
by the Bank prior to commencement of bidding procedures. The services
required to design the residential infrastructure and community facilities,
and supervise construction are included under this component of the
project, whether performed by the local authority staff, UDCORP. or
consultants engaged by the towns.
2.13     Housing Finance.  Under the project, the building societies will
provide the necessary finance for both plot purchase and dwelling unit
construction. As noted above, the building societies became involved in
the First Urban project by providing low-cost housing finance, and their
involvement will be greatly expanded in the proposed project.  Housing
finance will be provided by them for both the plot acquisition price and
house construction cost in the amount of Z$ 460 million. This is
sufficient to cover all of the mortgage requirements anticipated under the
project. The average loan is expected to be Z$ 10,000, including the
estimated purchase price of the plot(see para. 4.22). During negotiations,
agreement was reached that Government would ensure that the building
societies utilize lending criteria consistent with those agreed between the
Government and the Bank for allocations of mor'gage loans.  The building
societies would provide 1002 of the financing required for this component,
utilizing funds raised through their normal operatione (see paras. 4.30 to
4.35).
2.14     The introduction of the building societies into Government's low
cost housing program under Urban I has been a major factor in reducing the
financial burden on the public sector. In addition to the building
societies providing 100? of the capital required to finance house
construction costs under the project, a steady supply of serviced sites
will be assured by the proposed sector loan. This will facilitate a
restructuring of the financing of the low cost housing that will involve
the complete replacement of government funds by those of the private sector
for house construction costs (see paras. 4.23 to 4.25).   It will also
allow the partial replacement of Government funds by those of the building
societies for the construction costs of the serviced sites.  This would be
achieved by the establishment of revolving funds in each participating
local authority, built on the benefits of the differential between the
long-term financing provided by the NHF for servicing the plots and the
short-term return on this investment to the local authorities realized from
the sale of the plots. The revolving funds, if properly managed, could
become a steadily expanding source of funds for housing infrastructure
development, as an alternative to Central Government transfers.
2.15     The operation of a revolving fund (see Annex 9) in a local
authority will depend on five key elements: (i) the continued availability
to the local authority of long-term loans from NHF; (ii) a regular supply
of serviced sites, in order for funds to be recycled on a steady.
continuing basis; (iii) a short period of time (two to three years) between
the commencement of construction of infrastructure and the receipt of
payments for plots from the building society as it provides mortgages to
the household beneficiaries; (iv) the type of cost recovery mechanisms in
place which will affect the plot price (the amount of land servicing costs



- 18 -
recovered in the plot price); and (v) the establishment of the tightly
controlled rec-nling procedures necessary to a well managed revolving fund
in the local authority. Thus, while Government would be required, during
the first three years, to invest substantial funds, by the end of the third
year a major portion of the initial year's funding would be returned to the
revolving fund mnd would be available for reinvestment.
2.16     Thereafter, the demands on Central Government could be reduced
taking into account the following: (i) the difference between the cost of
servicing plots and the selling price of the plots (estimated at about 302
of the plot cost);2 (ii) an annual topping up of the revolving fund, based
on the rate of urban population growth pluis inflation; and (iii) local
authority amortization of the long-term NHF and GDLF loans. During
negotiations, agreement was reached that each local authority participating
in the project would be required to establish a revolving fund from the
proceeds of plot sales, in accordance with conditions and procedures
acceptable to MPCNH and the Bank.
2.17     During appraisal, the adequacy of funds available to the building
societies and hence the capacity of these societies to provide the
requisite mortgage financing under the proposed project were estimated to
be sufficient. However, during implementation the flow of such funds will
be monitored to determine whether any further measures are required to
facilitate either (i) the flow of additional funds to the societies (see
para. 4.34) or (ii) the flow of funds from them for housing loans under the
project. Monitoring will also be required to ensure that the funds
available for mortgage financing are well distributed among income groups
according to criteria agreed on during appraisal (see para. 3.10).
2.18     In order to ensure that the building societies can effectively
manage the significant increase in the number of mortgages to be originated
under the program, and to facilitate the expansion of their mobilization of
savings deposits, the project will provide financing to all three societies
for the foreign currency necessary to acquire addit.onal computing
equipment (estimated at Z$ 12.0 million). The on-lending terms and
conditions of these loans were agreed on during negotiations, and
Government would obtain assurances that the building societies commit a
minimum 252 of their total annual dollar volume of lending to the sector
program, for mortgages to low income households (that is families with
household incomes of Z$650 per month or less) according to the proportions
described in para. 3.10. In addition, Government would obtain satisfactory
assurances that the buiilding societies make suitable provisionF, under the
sector program, for the sepply of mortgage loans for the temaining 302 of
plot allocations, as ina.cated in para. 3.10, to moderate/louer-middle
income beneficiaries whose hout?hold incomes range between Z$650 and Z$1000
per month. The format for monitoring lending performance by the building
societies that was prepared and agreed on during appraisal, (see Annex 10)
was confirmed during negotiations. It was agreed during appraisal that, in
the event of excessive delays by the building societies, (12 months or more
2/ This does not represent a subsidy of the plot purchase price, but
comprises those elements of the plot which are recovered through pricing,
as opposed to other capital costs which are recovered through tariffs (see
pares. 4.22 and 4.23).

from time of submission of a fully complete mortgage applicatiosi to time of
approval and offer of a mortgage), alternative means for financing the
purchase of the plot and constructing the superstructure would be sought.
In order to ensure the competitiveness of the building societies in
mobilizing savings, it was agreed during appraisal, and confirmed during
negotiations, that an annual evaluation of their performance, in the
context of the project, would be undertaken by Government. This review
would be -haired by MPEPD, and its results and any actions proposed to be
taken, would be communicated to the Bank for comment.
Regional Development Program
2.19     The regional development program will operate at two levels.  One
will concentrate on the development role of the secondary towns along the
Bulawayo-Harare-Mutare Corridor, including the Greater Harare Metropolitan
Area. The other will focus on the development, on a pilot basis, of
relatively small growth centers in the context of the region within which
each center is situated. Particular attention will be paid to
strengthening the role played by the towns, cities and growth centers, in
supporting employment creation/income generation opportunities within their
own areas. The project will operate by making available to the local
authorities, through MLGRUD, qualified professional staff with appropriate
expertise and experience and by providing, on a pilot basis, for strategic
capital investments (such as infrastructure for small enterprise estates),
as they are identified in the small regional growth centers, to support
priority development programs.
2.20     Preparation of the Combination Development Plan and Investment
Strategy for the Greater Harare Metropolitan Area. For some time it has
been apparent that with the population growth pressures concentrated in
Harare and its resultant expansion towards Chitungwiza and several other
smaller local authorities, an integrated development strategy and
investment program is required for the Harare region. Terms of reference
have been prepared by MLGRUD and were agreed during appraisal. The project
would provide technical assistance to MLGRUD in the form of a consultant
team. The consultants would be fully integrated with the combination
development plan team which has already been established. An estimated 9
staff years of technical assistance (Z$ 1.7 million) is required. Included
in this program would be the development and implementation of information
management systems in Harare (Z$ 0.4 million).
2.21     Development of Secondary Cities and Towns along the
BulawayolHarare/Mutare Corridor. The secondary towns included in this sub-
component are Gweru, Kwekwe/Redcliff, Kadoma, Chegutu, Mutare and
Marondera, all of which are located along the main transport artery and
industrial axis of the country. They are important industrial, commercial
and service centers and have a strong influence on national economic
growth, ;patial distribution of economic activity, and urbanization trends.
As counter poles to Harare and Bulawayo, these towns are expected to grow
as employment centers, thus causing some moderation in the population
growth of the larger cities. The project will provide technical assistance
to further strengthen this ro:e of the corridor towns through the
preparation of a Corridor Town Development Plan and Investment Strategy.
The strategy would include a particular focus on employment creation/income



- 20 -
generation by promoting the expanaion of existing industrial and commercial
activities and attracting new ventures, as well as supporting the
development of the informal sector. Infrastructure and housing will be
available under the subsector loans to support this component. The
technical assistance would comprise 15 staff years (Z$ 2.8 million) and
vehicles and equipment to facilitate operations of the technical assistance
program (Z$ 0.2 million). A study for appraising the small scale/micro and
informal enterprise (SSE) sector would be included under this component and
would examir... zhe structure of SSEs in Zimbabwe, and the opportunities and
constraints to their development and growth (Z$ 0.4 million). Terms of
reference for the technical assistance and the study were prepared and
agreed during appraisal.
2.22     Development of Regional Growth Centers.  The regional growth
center program is based on the principle that the role of the small
centers, in providing essential urban services, also contributes to the
development of the economic potential of the region within which they are
located. In order to determine the most efficient allocation of investment
resources, the project would support the preparation of District
Development Investment Plans on a pilot basis in two provinces (Mashonaland
East and Manicaland). The plans would focus on the role of selected
centers in promoting district development and the linkages of the centers
to their hinterlands, including improvements to the towns as market
centers. Towns with demonstrated growth potential have been chosen.
Special emphasis would be placed on supporting income generation and the
development of employment opportunities, particularly amongst micro-
enterprise/informal sector activities. The growth centers identified for
this sub-component include Mrewa and Mutoko in Mashonaland East, and
Rusape, Murambinda, Chipinge and Nyanga in Manicaland. The project would
provide 15 staff years of technical assistance (Z$ 2.8 million) and related
vehicles and equipment (Z$ 0.2 mill4on).  A fund for financing pilot
capital investments that are considered to have strategic benefits to
employment promotion has also been included (Z$ 11.63 million). Terms of
reference for the technical assistance to be provided under this sub-
component were prepared and agreed during appraisal.
Institutional Development
2.23     Strengthening the Management of Public Investments in the Urban
Sector. At the Central Government level, responsibility for review of
annual capital development proposals by the urban councils under the PSIP
process rests with MLGRUD. The project would strengthen the capacity of
the Ministry to undertake a more comprehensive appraisal of these proposed
investments through technical assistance to, and training of staff in, all
relevant departments. In addition, the technical assistance would provide
for a certain amount of in-service training to the local authorities (see
paras. 2.26 and 3.12). A total of 25 staff years (covering 3 financial
analysts and 2 municipal engineers) would be provided (Z$ 4.8 million),
together with the vehicles and equipment required to support the technical
assistance (2$ 0.5 million).
2.24     Similarly, investments in housing and housing-related
infrastructure are the responsibility of MPCNH which appraises annual
proposals by the local authorities under the PSIP. The capacity of MPCNH
to undertake the technical evaluation of urban housing investment proposals



- 21 -
is also to be strengthened under the project through the provision of 9
staff years (Z$ 1.7 million) of technical. assistance (civil engineer,
architectiplanner and quantity surveyor). In addition vehicles and
equipment are to be provided (ZS 0.2 million). The project will also
finance a study (Z$ 0.1 million), to be directed by MPCNH, of alternative
housing types and densities, and of different options for urban expansion
and diversification that would be facilitated by the development of a range
of housing systems (see para. 3.19). This is in response to growing
conc(erns in Government that there is a need to improve land use standards
in order to achieve greater efficiencies in urban transport and
infrastructure provision. Terms of reference for the technical assistance
and the studies were prepared and agreed during appraisal.
2.25     Strengthening the Institutional Capacity of Local Authorities.  As
part of its program of strengthening local authority financial management,
and in anticipation of the financial consequences likely to result from the
significant future increase in demand for investment in urban services,
MLGRUD has initiated a process requiring, with MLGRUD assistance,
preparation of a FPP by each local authority seeking to borrow funds
through the GDLF. The role of the FPPs and their farmat and content were
agreed during appraisal and confirmed during negotiations. It was also
agreed, and confirmed during negotiations, that all of the twenty-one towns
reviewed to date, would be required to submit complete individual FPPs
satisfactory to MLGRUD and the Bank, by November 30, 1990. The format for
the FPP is included at Annex 11, and completed FPPs for Gweru and Bindura
have been included at Annexes 12 and 13 as examples. Host of the towns
have commenced preparation of their plans and a schedule was agreed on
during appraisal for completing them (see Annex 14). This was confirmed
during negotiations. Integration of the FPP with the appraisal and PSIP
review cycle is described in paras. 3.05 and 3.06 A more detailed
description of the FPPs and their role in the management of local authority
finances is included in para. 4.12.
2.26     The introduction of financial management plans would be
complemented by local authority finance policy studies and training
programs for the local authorities, to be financed under the project, in
order to ensure availability of adequate skills at the management,
supervisory and operational (technician and clerical) levels. Such
training and assistance would be provided as follows: (i) the specialists
provided under paras. 2.23 and 2.24 above would assist local authorities in
preparing their FPPs and their submissions for appraisal under the PSIP
process. This assistance would also include some in-service training in
financial management, internal auditing and accounting procedures; (ii)
funds, on a grant basis, to the local authorities to employ UDCORP or other
local consulting firms to assist with the preparation of the FPPs and
undertake any remedial accounting and systems development/application work
that may be required (four staff years of technical assistance; Z$ 0.6
million); (iii) studies, for which two staff years of technical assistance
are included (Z$ 0.2 million), to improve Government's policy framework for
monitoring local authority financial performance and for determining
specific circumstances under which local authorities would be eligible for
assistance (Annex 15 sets out the studies); and (iv) a sector-wide local
authority training program covering all aspects of local authority
operations (technical/engineering, finance, administration, social
services/housing) at management and functional levels.



- 22 -
2.27     The scope, terms of reference and size of the training program
(para. 2.26 (iv) above) are to be determined by a training needs
assessment, financed by SIDA, that is to commence in June 1989. An
indicative estimate of 18 staff years (Z$ 3.4 million) has been included,
with an additional Z$ 0.4 million for vehicles and equipment. This would
be modified based on the findings of the assessment.
2.28     Program Coordination and Monitoring.  The Program Coordination and
Monitoring Unit (PCMU) established under Urban I will be retained under the
project and expanded to reflect the more extensive scope of the sector
project. An additional municipal engineer and accountant will be
recruited, and a planner/housing specialist will be added to the staff
resulting in a total staff complement of two municipal engineers, one
planner/housing specialist, and three accountants/financial analysts.
Either the more senior municipal engineer or the planner will be designated
as Program Coordinator, in the event that a senior official from either
MLGRUD or MPCNH cannot be seconded to this position. Support staff (three
secretaries) will also be provided. A total of 20 staff years is to be
provided for the PCMU (Z$ 3.8 million) as well as vehicles and equipment
(2$ 0.2). Terms of reference were prepared and agreed during appraisal.
2.29     Strengthening of Urban Transport Planning and Management.  The
Urban Transport Unit was set up under Urban I within the Department of
Physical Planning and has proven effective in guiding urban transport
policy and the urban transport program of MLGRUD. Local staff have also
been trained under Urban I and now occupy key positions in the Unit.
However, the Unit will require additional technical assistance and training
until it is fully staffed, and seven staff years of assistance is to be
provided under the project (Z$ 1.3 million) together with vehicles and
equipment (Z$ 0.1 million).   Terms of reference for the technical
assistance requirements of UTU were prepared and agreed during appraisal.
D. Project Costs
2.30     The estimated total cost of the project including taxes and duties
is Z$ 1,149.5 million (US$580 million), of which US$114.5 million
equivalent or 202 is foreign exchange. Total project costs include land
costs of Z$ 1.8 million (US$0.9 million). Table 2.1 presents a summary
breakdown of the costs for the basic project components (see Annex 16,
Table 1 for detailed cost estimates). Base costs are in June 1989 prices
and include design and supervision costs of Z$ 29.8 million (US$15.7
million). Taxes and duties are estimated at Z$ 65.5 million (US$33.1
million). Cost estimates are based on the assumption that the project
would be executed during the period 1990-1996. Cost estimates for the
primary infrastructure and housing components of the project, which
comprise the subsector loans and represent the major share of total project
costs, are derived from expenditure levels based on past investment
programs, as well as taking into account crrrently available preliminary
engineering designs for the first two years of the program. The land cost
for the housing program will be the bid price for proposed land to be
acquired. Project mortgage amounts are estimated from expected sales
prices of serviced plots, less downpayment on land developed under the
project for residential purposes, and on estimates of material and labor
costs, less downpayments, for house construction costs in the project
areas.



- 23 -
2.31     The advisory services and training activities to be provided by
specialist are estimated to cost about US$8,000 per man-month (inclusive of
housing allowances and transport). Training costs, to be based on actual
costs of training programs identified, will be finalized following
completion of the training needs assessment (see para. 2.27). Design,
supervision and management costs range from 42 to 122, depending on the
extent and repetitiveness of the works involved.
2.32     Physical contingencies are estimated at 102 of base costs
(including design and supervision, but excluding land, mortgage amounts,
and technical assistance and training). Price contingencies on costs of
imported vehicles, equipment, materials and internationally recruited staff
and consultants have been included at annual rates of 32 p.a. in FY 1990
and 42 p.a. thereafter. In the case of local costs, price contingencies
were calculated based on the following factors: 112 in FY 1990 and 102 p.a.
thereafter; these rates reflect the inflationary trends in overall
construction costs in 1987-88. Price increases, for local costs, largely
in the construction sector, are expected to exceed increases in the general
level of prices during the project period. The total price contingencies
amount to about 212 of base costs including physical contingencies but
excluding housing finance. To convert local prices to dollar equivalents,
it is assumed that the divergence between local and foreign price
escalation will be converted by exchange rate adjustments over the life of
the '-oject.

million) would finance about 17.52 of the total project costs. Bilateral
donors would provide the equivalent of US$3.0 million (Z$ 5.7 million) as
grants (0.52). Government funds would provide Us$234 million equivalent
(Z$ 483.6 million) or 402 of total project costs and the building societies
the equivalent of US$242 million (Z$ 460.0 million) or 422 of total project
costs. The financing plan is summarized in Table 2.2.

2.34     Cofinancing is to be provided as follows:
(a)  Grant funding for the local authority training component,
under assistance from SIDA (US$3.0 million). This would be
based on (i) the outcome of the training needs assessment
which is being financed by SIDA; and (ii) a review of the
next three-year program of institution development assistance
by SIDA to Zimbabwe, which is expected to be finalized by
June 30, 1990. SIDA funding for the local authority training
program, if approved, would be on a parallel-financing basis.
(b)  Soft loan support for civil works (US$21.0 million) which is
under review fo?. funding by the Ministry of Economic
Cooperation of the Federal Republic of Germany and the
Kreditanstalt fur Wiederaufbau (KfW). A formal decision is
expected to be taken by June 30, 1990.
