In the event that SIDA financing is not available, Government funds would
be utilized to provide the additional US$3.0 million required for the local
authority training program. If KfW funding for civil works under the
sector loan is not approved, the sector program would be correspondingly
reduced by US$21.0 million.
Flow of Funds and Onlending .rrangements
2.35     The IBRD loan of US$ 80 million would be made available to GOZ at
the standard IBRD terms with a maturity of 20 years. The proceeds of the
IBRD loan would be passed on to the various implementing agencies as loans
according to prevailing practices, with the exception of the pilot capital
development fund for the regional development component (US$6.1 million),
which would be provided on a grant basis as a budgetary allocation to
MLGRUD. Vehicles and equipment (financed out of the proceeds of the loan)
linked to the technical assistance and training components of the project
would be made available by Government as budgetary allocation (MLGRUD and
MPCNH and grants (local authorities). All technical assistance and
training under the project that is to be cofinanced by donors, would be on
a grant basis and would be passed on to the implementing agencies as
budgetary allocations (MLGRUD and MPCNH) and grants (local authorities).
2.36     The proceeds of the IBRD loan together with the Government's own
contribution to the project will be made available, as shown in Chart 1, as
follows:
(a)  to the GDLF in MLGRUD and the NHF in MPCNH, and then onlent
as follows 11) funds for primary infrastructure, urban
service mairntenance-related vehicles, plant and equipment,
community facilities, and traffic management improvements
would be onlent to the local authorities through the GDLF;
(ii) funds for on-site infrastructure for housing purposes
would be onlent to the local authorities through the NHF;
(b)  as loan from MFEPD to the building societies for computer
equipment; and
(c)  as a loan from MFEPD to the Zimbabwe Electricity Supply
Authority (ZESA).
2.37     The terms and conditions of the onlending of funds to (i) the
local authorities would be in accordance with prevailing terms of the GDLF
and the NHF (see paras. 4.16 to 4.18), for a period of 25 years including a
grace period of 3 years; (ii) the building societies would be on commercial
lending terms including interest at the prevailing prime rate plus a
percentage to cover the foreign exchange risk; and (iii) ZESA would be in
accordance with prevailing terms to parastatals, currently 9.752 per annum,
payable in 20 years including a grace period of 5 years. Subsidiary loan
agreements satisfactory to the Bank, between Government and each of the
implementing agencies (eligible local authorities, ZESA and the three local
authorities) would be required in order for disbursements to be made
against expenditures incurred under the project by each agency. The
foreign exchange risk would be borne by the Government. The terms and
conditions of the on-lending arrangements were reviewed and agreed on
during negotiations.

2.38     Plot/house loans to project beneficiaries would be provided by the
building societies on the following terms: the prevailing market rate of
Jnterest, currently 12.5?, a grace period of six to nine months and a term
to maturity of 25 years. The interest rate would be adjusted periodically
to reflect prevailing financial and economic conditions, following current
practice of the building societies.
F. Environmental Impact
2.39     Zimbabwe has long recognized the need to protect the watercourses
which provide the country's drinking water and the project will seek to
maintain the high standard achieved, and introduce improvements where
required. Standards for discharge of waste into watercourses are strict
and most of the country's towns have satisfactory sewerage systems; some
low density areas have septic tanks rather than sewers. Sewage is treated
either in oxidation ponds or with other appropriate methods including
activated sludge processes. Under the project, the continued use of these
environmentally sound methods will be encouraged.
2.40     The primary infrastructure component of the project includes
substantial new investment in sewerage and sewage treatment facilities to
ensure that the increased generation of wastewater resulting from both
housing and industrial development will be properly collected via
waterborne sewerage systems and treated to the required standards. Where
industrial wastewater causes particular problems (e.g. in Chitungwiza), new
treatment facilities are to be installed. In Victoria Falls, where sewage
now collected from the town via a waterborne sewerage system is discharged
(after screening only) directly into the Zambezi River, the HLGRUD has been
pressing the Town Council to construct proper treatment facilities. Under
the project, the local authority will be able to carry out the installation
of these facilities.
2.41     Vehicles and equipment to be provided under the project will
assist in addressing environmental problems in a number of ways. One of
these problems is the shrinking and deteriorating vehicle fleets that have
degraded the quality of solid waste collection services. New vehicles and
plant to be provided through the project will help improve the collection
and disposal of waste (in sanitary landfill) and contribute to better urban
services.
2.42     The proposed housing program will support efforts to accommodate
the rapidly growing urban population in properly planned environments,
serviced with water and sewerage reticulation, roads, electricity,
lighting, and surface-water drainage. New housing developments will be
located in generally flat and clear terrain and such growth should not
result in environmental damage. Where the expansion of town areas is
required for housing development, farms are usually acquired and the
existing trees or small wooded areas are normally well-preserved in
proposed housing layouts; these areas with trees are often allocated to
public parks and other community purposes. In no instance will
resettlement of existing populations be required to accommodate new
development.



- 29 -
2.43     In a number of towns, there are currently some densely populated
and poorly-serviced housing areas (e.g. Epworth in Harare, and parts of
Redcliff, Kariba and Shurugwi). These particular residential areas will be
improved through abandonment of communal toilet facilities and communal
water points and the provision of more satisfactory substitute facilities.
Among the benefits from these changes will be the reduction of water losses
and the prevention of serious pollution now caused by overloaded septic
tanks.
G. Impact on Women
2.44     The availability of low-cost housing not only improves women's
access to housing but also relieves them of some of the pressure to supply
supplemental income to the household, a burden that can be severe when they
are caring for small children. Their limited earnings are chiefly derived
from work in the informal sector because men are generally preferred over
women for wage employment. Yet women's earnings are often essential to
their family's ability to obtain satisfactory housing.
2.45     For the female head of household, and there are many of them in
Zimbabwe, access to low-cost housing is of particular significance in view
of their usually precarious financial circumstances and economic
vulnerability. Under the housing project, as has been evidenced in the
plot and mortgage allocation procedures under Urban I, they will have
access to available housing equal to that of other household applicants
including access to credit and support programs.
2.46     Many rural women, both married and single, seek to improve their
economic situation or living conditions by moving from communal areas to
urban centers. A major deterrent to such relocation, however, is a severe
shortage of urban housing. Often a husband will leave his rural home and
work in an urban area for an extended period until he can find housing that
will enable the family to join him in the city. The availability of new
urban housing, as provided under the project, will thus facilitate the
desired relocation of many women. This opportunity will mean a substantial
easirng of the extreme workload that women traditionally bear in rural areas
since they are generally responsible for the heavy and time-consuming
burden of carrying water and firewood, often from distant sources of
supply, to their homes.



- 30 -
III. IMPLEMENTATION OF PROJECT
A. Institutional Arrangements
Overview
3.01     The experience of the Government of Zimbabwe in implementing the
First Urban Development Project demonstrated the considerable strength of
existing institutions, Not only have local authorities carried out their
functions under the project efficiently, but also the central Government
has effectively coordinated and monitored implementation, integrating the
investment requirements of the project with the annual PSIP exercise. The
establishment and operation of a Steering Committee to provide policy
guidance with project execution, comprising senior officials from the
implementing ministries and MFEPD, and a Program Coordination and
Monitoring Unit (PCMU), based in MLGRUD but responsible to the Steering
Committee, to monitor day-to-day operations, also contributed to the
satisfactory performance under Urban I.
3.02     Recognizing the complexity of the proposed project and the
relatively simple implementation arrangements as evidenced under Urban I,
maximum use is to be made of existing institutions and established
procedures. The local authorities will assume the primary role in project
execution, having responsibility for planning, designing and constructing
all civil works and coAmunity facilities which comprise the major part of
the investment to be funded through Bank assistance. As previously noted,
financing provided to the local authorities will flow through the GDLF and
che NHF while financing of plot purchases and house construction will be
provided and managed by the building societies in close cooperation with
the local authorities, particularly in the area of allocation procedures.
The latter functions will be monitored by MPCNH and the PCMU. Management
and coordination of implementatiorn of local authority training and
technical assistance activities will be the responsibility of MLGRUD. The
electricity facilities component of the project will be implemented by
ZESA. Regional development will be implemented mainly by MLGRUD's
Department of Physical Planning which will be strengthened with technical
assistance support.
3.03     Under the proposed project, the overall monitoring of the various
project activities will continue to be undertaken by the PCHU, assisted by
MPCNH on the housing component, and facilitated by the Steering Committee,
which will comprise the permanent secretaries (or their designees) from
each of the two implementing ministries and MFEPD. This committee, which
will provide oversight and general guidance on project implementation, will
be chaired by the Permanent Secretary, MLGRUD, with the Program Coordinator
serving as Secretary. The PCMU will report directly to the Chairman of the
Steering Committee to ensure that project implementation will be carried
out in accordance with the Loan Agreement. During negotiations, these
arrangements, including the staffing of the PCMU were reviewed and agreed.



- 31 -
Sector Loan Procedures
3.04     The PSIP system utilized by Government for determining the scope
and scale of the annual urban infrastructure and housing program was
reviewed during appraisal (see paras. 4.14 and 4.15) and was found to be a
reasonably thorough, equitable, orderly, and timely process with reliable
checks and balances on both the types and levels of investments being
proposed by the local authorities. Accordingly, and taking into account
progress under Urban I, there is sufficient experience to initiate a sector
lending approach to urban service and housing developments. Some further
strengthening of the key organizational entities, both at the center
(MLGRUD and MPCNH) and amongst the local authorities, is to be provided
under the project, particularly to enhance their capability to undertake
appraisals of specific investment proposals (see paras. 2.23, 3.12, and
3.17).  During negotiations, a schedule for recruiting the technical
assistance for MLGRUD and MPCNH by December 31, 1989 was reviewed and
agreed.
3.05     Given the satisfactory existing system, it was concluded that the
appraisal of investments under the sector loan should be carefully
integrated into the annual budgetary cycle, strengthening without modif)ing
existing PSIP procedures. The sector loan approach, in introducing a more
vigorous appraisal performance, is expected to provide significant
benefits, including the following: (i) substantial improvements in
evaluation by the local authorities of the technical feasibility of
proposals. In particular, this would contribute to the development of
least cost solutions and, based on more extensive feasibility analysis
requirements, would ensure greater cost-efficiencies in the scheduling of
investments; (ii) more effective prioritization between competing
investments and between competing local authorities; (iii) better
coordination and balance between inter-dependent investments (for example,
excess capacity in water treatment in relation to transmission capacity; or
excess waste treatment capacity in relation to treated water
supply/consumption); (iv) introduction of economic indicators of rates of
return as a factor in investment decisions; and (v) introduction of the FPP
which would serve as the key instruments in determining the affordability
of proposed investments as measured against existing and projected
financial performance by each local authority, and would consequently
facilitate greater discipline in expenditures and improved financial
management controls.
3.06     The procedures that were agreed during the appraisal mission for
undertaking appraisals of proposed projects and integrating them with the
PSIP are described in Annex 17 and are to be circulated by MFEPD through
MLGRUD and MPCNH, for primary infrastructure and housing respectively, to
the local authorities as part of the annual PSIP package. In addition, the
package would include a summary of the appraisal criteria to be utilized in
evaluating submissions under the PSIP, as agreed during appraisal (see
Annex 18). Attached to the appraisal criteria are detailed guidelines for
preparing the requisite technical and financial information. An
information sheet designed to augment MPCNH's existing PSIP format was
prepared and agreed during appraisal and is set out in Annex 19. MPCNH
would attach this sheet to the PSIP package they circulate to the local
authorities for preparation of the annual submissions. The appraisal
procedures and criteria agreed during appraisal were confirmed at
negotiations.



- 32 -
3.07     For all infrastructure and housing projects financed under the
sub-sector loans, MLGRUD and MPCNH would complete and retain Project
Appraisal Summaries, copies of which would be provided to the PCMU. These
summaries would be subject to a posteriori review by the Bank, on request,
on a sample basis. For those individual projects estimated to cost more
than US$5.0 million (excluding the cost, in the case of housing projects,
of house construction), the appraisal conducted by MLGRUD or MPCNH would be
forwarded to the Bank by the respective Ministry in the form of a Project
Appraisal Summary, through the PCMU, for review prior to approval and
inclusion under the sector loan. During negotiations, it was agreed that
MLGRUD would forward Project Appraisal Summaries. to the Bank for review and
approval for any project costing more than US$S.0 million.
Local Authorities
3.08     The 21 local authorities currently approved for participation in
the project differ somewhat in status (Cities, Municipal Councils, Town
Councils, and Town Boards), but in general provide similar basic community
services to the residents within their respective areas. The various
functions of the city and town administrations are carried out through
departments, of which the Engineers (or Works), Treasurers, and Community
Services (including housing) play a primary role in the design, preparation
and implementation of the project. Current capacities for detailed project
preparation and execution were evaluated and are considered generally
adequate, although further strengthening, particularly with the more
thorough project appraisal standards being introduced under the project, is
to be accomplished indirectly through the technical assistance to MLGRUD
and MPCNH and directly through the local authority training program (see
paras. 3.12 and 3.13). The local authorities have also relied,
traditionally, on the consulting services of the private sector, which are
well developed in Zimbabwe, and this practice, which has proven effective,
is expected to continue under the project. The services of UDCORP will
further add to the options available to the local authorities for
consultant support. In order to ensure coordination of implementation and
monitoring of progress, each local authority would establish a Working
Committee with at least one representative from each of the above three
departments serving on the committee, and would designate one of the
members as Project Coordinator.
3.09     The community services departments will assume responsibility for
administering and implementing the housing component of the project on
completion of civil works. This will include beneficiary selection and
plot allocation, in conjunction with the building societies, assistance to
the beneficiaries with building their housing, and inspection (together
with the department of works, engineering or building and amenities) of
construction to ensure that appropriate standards are met. The typical
organizational structures of two different sized local authorities (Harare
and Bindura) are diagramed in Annex 20, Charts 20.1 and 20.2 respectively.
3.10     Housing allocation procedures will be itn accordance with current
practices under the National Housing Fund (which are utilized under Urban I
and are satisfactory to the Bank), which are based on local authority
waiting lists, that are updated annually. It was confirmed at negotiations



- 33 -
that, in terms of 1989 incomes, 70X of the plots would be sold to low-
income households; that is households with incomes below Z$650 per month
(of these, 60% would be for households with incomes below Z$450 per moath,
and 40% would be for households with monthly incomes between Z$450 and
Z$650). The remaining 301 of the plots developed under the project would
be allocated to moderatellower-middle income households earning between
Z$650 and Z$1000 per month. Thesa income ceilings would be subject to
revision from t4me-to-time in line with changes in incomes And prices, and
subject to Bank review and confirmation. No minimum income limit would be
set, but affordability in each case would be evaluated in terms of income,
size of unit to be built (one room plus abluticn is considered a minimum
initial provision to permit plot occupation), amount of personal savings to
be utilized, and specific information on or evidence of mode of
construction to be employed (informal contractors and payment in kind
considerably reduce construction costs). Income from renting of rooms will
also be taken into account where appropriate. Applicants satisfying the
above criteria who require mortgage loans will be allocated a plot
provisionally, subject to approval of a mortgage by the building society.
Based on consideration of the above, the amount of the mortgage loan will
be determined assuming that about 251 of income is required for servicing
the mortgage payments. During negotiations, assurances were obtained from
Government that in accordance with Government policy of self-managed
progressive house construction, one room plus ablution facility would be
the accepted minimum initial provision that would allow plot occupation and
would serve as the basis for evaluating aifordability when allocating plots
and mortgage loans.
Central Government
3.11     MLGRUD is responsible for all major programs (other than housing)
affecting local government administration throughout Zimbabwe, and all
aspects of local authority finance. The organization of MLGRUD is
diagramed in Annex 20. Chart 20.3 The five departments of the uinistry
with project-related responsibilities are Urban Councils, Rural Councils,
Financial Services, Local Government Promotion and Training, and Physical
Planning.
3.12     Primary responsibility in MLGRUD for the project appraisal and
PSIP evaluation process of all local authority investments proposed under
the sub-sector loan is to be shared by the Departments of Financial
Services, Urban Councils, and Rural Councils, with Financial Services
playing a coordinating role. The municipal engineers and financial
analysts to be provided the Ministry to assist with establishing and
operating the appraisal system (see para. 2.23) would be placed in the
Urban Councils Department (but be available to the Rural Councils
Department as needed) and the Financial Services Department respectively.
A major additional activity of these specialists would be in-service
support to the local authorities in preparing submissions for the annual
PSIP that would include proposed investment programs, feasibility studies
and technical studies for specific investments, as well as preparing or
updating the FPP. The local authorities and MLGRUD would follow the
appraisal process referred to in para. 3.06.
3.13     The local authority training program would be the responsibility
of the Local Government Promotion and Training Department of MLGRUD,



- 34 -
although most training activity is expected to be delivered by current
programs operating in the local authorities, and supported by the technical
assistance proposed under the project. In particular, this includes the
infrastructure maintenance and workshop-based training provided by Harare
City Council to all local authorities in Zimbabwe, and a finance training
program similarly located within one or two major city councils (Harare and
possibly Bulawayo). The technical assistance to be provided under the
project would be coordinated by the Local Government Promotion and Training
Department, but would probably be seconded, in large part, to the local
authorities providing the training. The eventual design of the training
program and the modalities and schedule for delivering it would be reviewed
and agreed on following completion of the training needs assessment being
financed by SIDA, that is referred to in paza. 2.27. The training program
would be coordinated and integrated with those support services provided by
UDCORP.
3.14     The regional development component would be implemented by the
various departments in MLGRUD, including Physical Planning, Urban Councils,
Rural Councils, and Rural Development, and with the relevant local
authorities. Existing planning procedures would be followed to ensure
appropriate coordination between the various sector agencies. The
implementation arrangements would also be coordinated with the available
skills in UDCORP.
3.15     The PCMU would monitor and coordinate progress with implementation
of all aspects of the project and would provide assistance. on a
contingency basis, where problems are being encountered. As part of its
responsibilities for monitorirg progress, the PCMU would also provide
support to strengthen the intormation management capabilities of MLGRUD,
MPCNH and the local authorities regarding investment programming and
monitoring of progress with project implementation. The PCMU would
coordinate GOZ inputs to the Project Completion Report, which should be
completed within six months of the project closing date. The Program
Coordinator, the senior municipal engineer, and the senior financial
analyst would be appointed before December 31, 1989. This schedule,
together with that for recruitment of other staff to the PCMU was confirmed
during negotiations. The PCMJ would also be responsible for preparing and
submitting to the bank, through MFEPD, all disbursement applications.
3.16     MPCNH.  The functions of MPCNH include: (i) setting natior.al
housing policies; (ii) ensuring that these policies are implemented by the
local authorities; (iii) controlling, through the NHF, the flow of finances
to the local authorities for housing-related infrastructure and housing
development in general; (iv) advising the Minister on proposals for low-
cost housing development; (v) reviewing and approving all housing
development projects, including the PSIP review process for housing
investment proposals; (vi) undertaking research to provide a basis for
sound housing policies and for assistance in housing and infrastructure
layout design and construction; and (vii) undertaking major low-cost
housing projects directly on behalf of local authorities (most likely in
rural areas) where this is deemed necessary. The organizational structure
of MPCNH is diagramed in Annex 20, Chart 20.4.



- 35 -
3.17     Primary responsibility for Central Government appraisal of housing
investments by the local authorities rests with the Strategic Planning
Branch, the Professional Services Division, and the Housing Policy Division
of MPCNH. The technical assistance to be provided by the Ministry under
the project (see para. 2.24) would be based in the Professional Services
Division, but would service the other two departments as needed in support
of their housing investment appraisal functions. Because of the linkage of
all housing investment to the availability of primary infrastructure, and
the role played by MLGRUD in determining the affordability of any proposed
investments to each local authority, MPCNH would be responsible for
coordinating its projected investment program with MLGRUD. This is
provided for in the appraisal procedures set out in Annex 17.
3.18     House construction, to be managed by the beneficiary, would be
based on a policy of progressive development and would be phased according
to ability to pay (see para. 3.11). During project implementation, the
Professional Services Division of MPCNH would monitor all aspects of the
housing component including preparation by the local authorities of the
housing section of the semi-annual progress reports. MPCNH would also
provide technical assistance to local authorities, as needed, with
preparation of housing programs, PSIP submissions and applications for use
of borrowing powers.
3.19     MPCNH is in the process of diversifying the range of housing types
it promotes under its housing program in order to improve efficiency,
increase density, provide for a wider range of options to the different
income groups, and improve the quality of the environment through greater
variety. To support this effort under the project, a short-term
consultancy would prepare a housing diversification program designed to
include a range of alternative area layouts, housing prototype designs and
higher densities. This work would be done under the MPCNH and the results
would then provide the basis for further elaboration by the Ministry,
utilizing in-house staff (including the technical assistance provided under
the project) and, where necessary, local consultants.
Building Societies
3.20     The building societies have well-established procedures for making
mortgage loans. These procedures have been followed under Urban I, but
with modifications being introduced gradually in response to the experience
gained in accommodating very high volumes of small loans. The revised
practices, while consistent with secure lending operations, provide for:
(i) improved coordination amongst key actors -- the local authorities,
MPCNH, the legal profession and the Surveyor-General; (ii) simplified
procedures for the beneficiary including the introduction of a "one-stop"
mortgage application/approval system; and (iii) rapid computerization,
including the development of custom software packages for loan processing.
During appraisal, these procedures were reviewed in detail and were
determined to be fully satisfactory. In order to ensure that an effective
mortgage allocation system is maintained, and to facilitate additional
efficiencies being introduced, it was agreed during appraisal, and this was
confirmed during negotiations, that progress review meetings would be held
at least bi-monthly, chaired by the Program Coordinator. The building
societies and the local authorities would maintain current reports on the



- 36 -
status of mortgage approvalldrawdown and plot allocation/house
construction, respectively, according to the formats set out in Annexes 10
and 21.
3.21     The mortgage loans to be provided by the building societies would
be sufficient to cover at least the purchase price of the plot plus the
cost of constructing a minimum shelter of one room and ablution facility.
The average mortgage for low-income owners is expected to be in the range
of ZS 5,000 - 8,000 and for medium-income owners Z$ 12,000 - 15,600. The
terms of the loan would be 25 years with a variable interest rate set at
the prevailing lower tier rate (currently 12.52) of the building societies.
B. Procurement
3.22     Contracts for civil works will be bulked together in bidding
packages to the maximum extent possible. However, the relatively small
size (the majority of the contracts do not exceed US$1.0 million),
scattered subproject locations (up to 50 local authorities) and staging of
contracts make it unlikely that foreign firms will be attracted. However,
a number of locally established foreign contractors in Zimbabwe should be
interested in bidding. All civil works contracts below US$1.5 million will
be awarded on the basis of LCB; foreign firms may participate in the
bidding. Nevertheless, because a number of contracts are expected to be
large, infrastructure works totalling about US$30.0 million are expected to
be procured through international competitive bidding in accordance with
Bank guidelines. There will be prequalification of firms for civil works
contracts, whether ICB or LCB is to be used, so that implementing agencies
can assess each firm's experience, financial standing, capacity, and
performance on similar construction works.
3.23     Because of their small size, most contracts for community
facilities will not be attractive to foreign firms. Therefore, contracts
for construction of community facilities totalling US$21.4 million will be
awarded on the basis of local competitive bidding procedures, which have
been reviewed and are satisfactory to the Bank. Foreign bidders may
participate.
3.24     Materials, equipment and labor for house construction totalling
US$242.0 million (all of which is financed by the building societies), will
be procured directly by individual households from local suppliers and
contractors.
3.25     Construction of certain infrastructure works and community
facilities by force account in the project towns will constitute about
US$30.0 million. This is considered an acceptable alternative to
competitive bidding because the works are small and scattered, in many
instances in remote locations where mobilization costs for contractors
would be unreasonably high. For some works, the quantities cannot be
defined in advance. In addition, experience under Urban I has shown that
qualified contractors may, in some cases not be interested. The works
would be carried out by the city or town engineer's department, which has
proven fully satisfactory under Urban I.
3.26     Equipment and vehicles totalling about US$33.0 million would be
grouped wherever possible into lots of at least US$250,000 and would be



- 37 -
procured through ICB. Items of equipment, materials, vehicles, spare parts
and supplies in lots of less than US$250,000 may be procured in accordance
with Government's standard procedures for local competitive bidding, which
were reviewed at appraisal and are considered acceptable.
3.27     US$3.0 million, to be provided by SIDA for technical assistrnce
and training, would be procured according to SIDA guidelines. Incremental
operating expenses (including staff salaries) would be paid directly under
government budgets.
3.28     Contracts for works exceeding US$1.5 million and for goods
exceeding US$250,000 will require prior Bank review of bidding
documentation and contract awards. The ceiling of US$1.5 million for civil
works is relatively high as LCB limits have normally been US$1.0 million.
However, the higher limit is being proposed because (i) experience under
Urban I has shown that contracts exceeding even this level have not been
attractive to foreign firms becavie of the capacity and competitive nature
of the construction industry in- Aimbabwe (under Urban I, which included a
large number of similar civil works contracts, none were awarded to
overseas contractors not represented locally, and very few submitted bids);
(ii) foreign firms are well represented locally and local bidding
procedures allow them to participate fully; and (iii) the geographically
dispersed location of many contracts would make ICB very difficult to
administer and unattractive to overseas firms. For all other contracts,
documentation will be forwarded to che Bank prior to submission of
disbursement applications. These procurement arrangements were confirmed
during negotiations.

C. Disbursements
3.29     Disbursements of the proceeds of the Bank loan would be made on
the following basis:
(a) 201 of total expenditures for all civil works; and
(b) 1002 of foreign expenditures and 50Z of locally procured
items for equipment and vehicles.
3.30     Disbursements would be made against standard Bank documentation
and, under category (a), would be made on the basis of statements of
expenditure for purchase orders, design and supervision, and works
implemented by force account. Disbursements under category (b) would be
made on the basis of statements of expenditure for contracts and purchase
orders with a total value less than US$100,000. A schedule of estimated
disbursement of the Bank loan is presented in Table 3.2. In order to
ensure the timely provision of funds available to finance the costs of
works and goods to be procured under the project, the Government would
establish a Special Account in the amount of US$4.0 million. Funds in the
Special Account would be available to finance only eligible expenditures
under the project. The arrangements for establishing and operating the
Special Account were agreed during negotiations. The project is expected
to be completed by June 30, 1996.

D. Accounts, Audits and Reporting Requirements
3.31     All institutions receiving or benefitting from Bank funds would
maintain, according to acceptable accounting standards, such financial
records as would enable all Bank funds disbursed for the project to be
clearly-identified and audited. The records should be such as to enable an
auditor to certify that Bank loans disbursed for the Project have been
spent on the purposes for which they were intended. The institutions
include, MLGRUD, MPCNH, ZESA, all participating local authorities, the
Project Monitoring Unit (PCMU), the building societies, and any other
entity receiving Bank funds. In addition to the above accounts, the PCMU
would also maintain a consolidated project account for all project
components, which would be included in the semi-annual progress reports
described in para. 3.34 below.
3.32     The separate project accounts maintained by MLGRUD and MPCNH would
be audited either by the Office of the Auditor-General or private auditors
appointed by his office, and this would be completed within nine months of
the end of the Government fiscal year. The project account audit reports
of MLGP.UD and MPCNH would also contain a separate opinion on the statement
of expenditure procedure.
3.33     In order to monitor the financial performance of the other
implementing agencies (the local authorities, ZESA and the building
societies), their financial statements would be audited by an independent
auditor acceptable to the Bank and the audit reports would be submitted to
the Bank within nine months of the end of their respective fiscal years.
The financial statements would separately identify project-related
expenditures. The audit report would contain a separate opinion on
compliance by the implementl.ag agencies with all financial covenants under
the Bank loan. It is expected that UDCORP would either carry out the
audits of the local authorities, or would appoint private auditors; these
arrangements are considered satisfactory. ZESA and the building societies
are expected to be audited by private auditors.
3.34     All project implementing institutions noted in para. 3.31 would
prepare progress reports on project implementation. The PCMU would monitor
progress with the reports and provide support to ensure completion of each
agency's report within one month of the end of each half-year. The format
for these reports will follow those utilized satisfactorily under Urban I.
Two additional forms will be added, one to be completed by the local
authorities to report on the status of housing development (see Annex 21),
the other to be completed by the building societies to report on the status
of mortgage loan allocations (see para. 2.18 and Annex 10). The PCMU will
synthesize these contributions into a consolidated semi-annual report that
will be forwarded to the Bank within two months of the end of the half-year
covered by the report.
3.35     During negotiations, assurances were obtained that all
implementing agencies would comply with the foregoing accounting, auditing,
and reporting requirements.



- 40 -
IV. FINANCIAL ANALYSIS
A. Local Authority Finance
Accounts and Budgets
4.01     Municipalities in Zimbabwe produce detailed accounts and budget
estimates and are responsible for maintaining strict control over
expenditures. Their accounting system is based on the income and
expenditure method used by local governments in the United Kingdom (UK).
Expenditure is accrued on both revenue and capital account, to the extent
that creditors who are paid up to three weeks following the end of the
fiscal year, are recorded in that fiscal year. In respect of debtors, all
known income is accrued, including rates due but not billed; and bad and
doubtful debts are written off annually. Each municipality is required to
prepare both revenue and capital budgets and a three.-year capital plan,
annually. These annual budgets are submitted to MLGhUD for review prior to
the start of the new financial year.
4.02     Municipal revenue budgets are divided into separate accounts, with
expenditures and income of each type charged to a particular account. In
general, each account is expected to be self-financing.
4.03     General administration expenses are funded out of two accounts;
the low density (middle and high income) areas are financed out of the
rates accounts while the high density (low and moderate income) areas are
financed out of what is now called the housing account or Local Government
Areas (LGA) account, for which the sources are mainly supplementary charges
and rents collected in these areas. Both the rates account and the various
housing (LGA) accounts also receive the proceeds of certain general
municipal taxes collected in their areas including fees paid by business
organizations for business licenses and fees paid by motor vehicle owners.
4.04     The separate financing for general administration purposes between
the rates account and the housing (LGA) account basically corresponds to
the division between high and low-income areas, a distinction carried over
from the past. This separation of financing has had the effect that the
areas with the greatest density and lowest incomes are largely supported by
the resources their particular areas can generate (excepting water,
sewerage, and electricity). Progress is being made by the local
authorities, albeit slowly, in implementing a "one-city" based accounting
system. Significantly, the city of Harare now merges the accounts. In
some cities, pending the implementation of the one-city concept of
accounting, any surplus from the rates fund is transferred to the housing
account to cover potential deficits in the latter.
4.05     The largest portion of municipal revenues flows through accounts
established for the purpose of financing (and collecting income from) the



- 41 -
provision of basic services. Each city has at least separate electricity1
and water accounts, and Harare has also established separate waste
management and sewerage accounts.  These accounts are funded by tariffs and
flat monthly charges levied by the city council and are intended to cover
the full cost of the services (see Annex 22 for a table of service charges
for 21 towns as of August 1988); the tariffs are generally revised as
necessary to keep abreast of changes in costs. In most cases, consumption
by the resident or customer is individually measured and billed.
4.06     Costs of social services in the high density areas are financed by
the beer account, which receives its revenues from the profits made by the
municipality on the manufacture and sale of beer. Up to 502 of the annual
profits from these beer sales may be used to finance low income housing, on
a revolving fund basis, while the rest must go for social services in the
current year. Any use of these funds which does not directly conform to
the established regulations must be approved by the MLGRUD.
Financial Status of the Local Authorities
4.07     Zimbabwe's cities have well-developed public services and their
financial performance or condition varies considerably; as indicated in
para. 1.21 there are some recent tendencies toward financial stress. The
principle of cost recovery is applied to services that can be run
commercially, such as water supply and waste disposal, but tariff increases
are sometimes delayed awaiting Central Government approval. Revenue
collections operate well and the accounts do not show any substantial
arrears. From 1980 to 1983, local government revenues, including small
transfers from Central Government, rose from Z$ 335.4 million to Z$ 675.1
million while total expenditures increased from Z$ 268.0 to Z$ 496.1
million and the current surplus increased from Z$ 67.4 million to Z$ 179.0
million. Subsequently, Central Government reduced its grants for education
and public health as responsibility for those functions was shifted to
local authorities.
4.08     A review of the income and expenditures of 21 local authorities
included under the project indicates that several of the towns had deficits
in their annual operating activity during FY 1988. These deficits have
been funded through various mechanisms including the drawdown of reserves
and balances, increasing the amounts owing creditors and the deferral of
repayments of loans made by the GDLF and the NHF. The deterioration of the
financial condition of many authorities has occurred recently and, although
the situation is not yet severe, the trend must be reversed. Annex 23
summarizes the trend in the annual recurrent budget surplus/deficit in the
21 selected local authorities. Generally, growth in revenues has fallen
short of increasing expenditures and few local authorities have been able
to accumulate surpluses. The rise in expenditures has resulted from a
1/ Recently, these electricity accounts were transferred to ZESA, along
with responsibility for providing reticulation services and power
generation under a restructuring of the power sector supported by the Bank
under the Zimbabwe Power I Project (ZIM 2212) and the Power Management
Assistance Project implemented by the Energy Sector Management Assistance
Program.



- 42 -
combination of factors including inflation, the increased demand for
services, and weakening of financial control. For 3 of the 21 councils,
five-year trend analyses of income and expenditures, including the level of
reserves is included as part of Annexes 5, 6, and 7.2   The FPP is to serve
as the means for reversing this recent decline in financial performance.
4.09     During the past five-year period, both the rate and housing (LGA)
accounts in the 21 towns taken together have had continuing deficits on an
annual basis. Aggregating accounts, of course, obscures particular cases
of good or bad performance. Generally, deficits in particular years have
been offset in subsequent years by increases in rates and supplementary
charges. In addition, cities have had to rely on financial surplu!es from
the accounts for water and beer undertakings to cover, in part, the
deficits in the rates and the housing accounts, at least on a temporary
basis until rents and rates were subsequently adjusted.
4.10     The deficits occur mainly because of delays in implementing
required increases in tariffs, rates, rents and supplementary charges to
meet steadily rising expenditures, delays caused to some extent by the
availability of funds from other sources. Over the years, tariffs have
changed only infrequently; recently, several councils have received MLGRUD
approval of substantial percentage increases in tariffs. Consistent with
the recommendations of the Bank's Urban Sector Report, under the Urban I
project Government initiated policies designed to rationalize housing
development and finance; these include local authorities financing only
plot development, and shifting responsibility for financing house
construction to the private sector. Another significant policy change by
MPCNH was the discontinuance of further provision of rental hrusing; thus,
home ownership is now emphasized and rental housing is becoming a
decreasing factor in municipal housing activity.
4.11     The aggregate debt of local authorities increased between 1980 and
1983 from Z$ 326.8 million to Z$ 562.7 million, or 72Z, a substantial rise
but a lesser percentage increase than that of Central Government debt which
rose by 1522 in the same period. The increased local government debt
service along with other cost increases impose a need for increasing
revenues. Clearly, the future financial viability of local authorities
will depend on their ability to expand their revenue base by increasing
rates and tariffs and identifying new sources of income. In th- past,
increases in rates and tariffs, to a large extent, kept pace with
increasing costs but this may become more difficult in the future due to
local pressures. The local authorities are acutely aware of this problem
and are attempting to design innovative measures to improve their financial
position. One significant step is that local authorities are attempting to
recover a higher percentage of infrastructure costs through plot sales
prices. The shift from local authority mortgage financing (including some
of the on-site infrastructure cost) to the building societies, as proposed
under the project, would substantially improve the prospects of financial
health of the cities in the future.
2/ Similar analyses are available in the Project Implementation File for
all 21 local authorities.



- 43 -
4.12     The FPP is being introduced as an instrument to assist in
improving the financial performance of local authorities. The FPP is
designed to provide details of each account operated by a local authority,
indicating the extent the account is in annual deficit (or surplus), the
status of debt servicing obligations, the requirements for increasing
sources of revenue in order to move from annual deficit to annual surplus
for each account, and the impact of any capital expenditure on revenue
generating requirements. The FPP is intended to operate over a three- to
five-year period, facilitating the shift from a deficit to a surplus
situation, while minimizing the hardship to the local authority. The FPP
consequently, serves as a planning instrument for the local authority,
whereby it can project annual targets for increases in revenue and link
these to ongoing debt servicing obligations, combined with proposed capital
development investments. The intention of the FPP is not necessarily to
preclude any capital expenditures under a deficit situation, but to make
sure that such expenditures are in the context of a plan (the FPP) that,
provided the expenditures are kept at planned levels and linked to a
program of increases in revenues, would result in the local authority
finances being in balance or surplus within the three- to five-year period.
A date for achieving this was discussed during appraisal. Agreement was
reached during negotiations that annual financial targets of all FPPs would
be designed to ensure that, at a minimum, on an annual basis, total
revenues for each of the principal service accounts shall be sufficient to
meet the following expenses: (i) for fiscal year 1991/92s total cash
operating expenses (excluding debt service); (ii) for fiscal year 1992/93:
the sum of total cash operating expenses and interest payments due; (iii)
for fiscal year 1993/94: the sum of total cash operating expenses and debt
service requirements; and (iv) for fiscal year 1994195: the sum of total
cash operating expenses and depreciation3 or debt service requirements,
whichever in the higher. The FPP would also enable MLGRUD to measure the
financial status of a local authority at any time and to evaluate whether
proposed submissions under the PSIP (or for other borrowings) are in line
with the targets agreed on between MLGRUD and the local authority, as
recorded in the FPP. The FPP would be modified as events dictate, and
would serve as a contract between MLGRUD and the local authority.
4.13     In order to facilitate the satisfactory completion of FPPs, it
will be necessary to ensure that the accounts of the participating local
authorities are up to date. Under the project, support for achieving this
is to be provided utilizing the technical assistance to MLGRUD, the local
authority training program, and the technical assistance earmarked
specifically for such activities as remedial accounting (see para.
2.26(ii). It was confirmed during negotiations that all past accounts
would be made current so that the accounts for FY89/90 for all the
participating local authorities could be closed by December 31, 1990 with
audits completed within nine months thereafter.
3/ or annual contribution to the capital development fund.



- 44 -
Sources of Development Funds
4.14     Central Government financial assistance to local authorities in
Zimbabwe is quite limited. There is no system of revenue sharing and there
are no subsidies from Central Government for local public services. For
capital projects, however, Government loans are made to local authorities,
primarily for general infrastructure works and for low-income housing
comprising mainly on-site infrastructure and community facilities. Funds
are earmarked during the budget cycle based on a project-by-project
evaluation by the Government. Thereafter, funds are released during the
fiscal year for those projects that have been approved. The Central
Government exercises a number of financial controls over local authorities
through MLGRUD, including approval of both budgets and the use of borrowing
powers; in the course of project appraisal, MLGRUD assesses the capacity of
local authorities to borrow the funds required for a particular project.
All borrowings by local authorities, whether from the Government or from
other non-local sources, must also be approved by MFEPD. All borrowing for
low-income housing infrastructure must now be financed through Government
loans. Loans from internal local authority sources (i.e., loans from one
special fund to another) remain under the control of the local authorities,
and are permitted so long as they comply with Government regulations.
4.15     Financial assistance to municipalities in the form of loans for
general infrastructure and for low cost housing, are controlled by MLGRUD
and MPCNH, respectively, which also consolidate the municipalities'
requests for lnan funds and serve as advocates for the municipalities in
the Central Government's budget and planning process. Requests for loans
through the GDLF and the NHF are approved in the yearly budget process of
the Central Government.
NHF
4.16     The NHF, under MPCNH, obtains its capital mainly as Government
appropriations and, occasionally (although not recently), as borrowings
from other institutions, particularly building societies and insurance
companies. TSae iHF is operated on a non-profit basis and its managers see&.
to recover only capital repayments and interest on its loans to local
authorities. The administrative costs, estimated at Z$ 51,000 per annum,
are included in the Ministry's overall administrative budget and are not
apportioned as costs chargeable to the Fund.
4.17     The current interest rate charged on the 25 year-loans made by the
NHF is 9.75Z per annum. Although, in theory, the lending rate must equal
the cost of borrowing (pooled" rate), the Fund's lending rate cannot be
adjusted without Treasury approval. In practice, the Fund has not always
passed on to its borrowers increases in its own cost of funds. As of June
30, 1988, the Fund had a deficit and the Treasury agreed to compensate the
Fund for this loss but to date no monies have been received from Treasury
against Fund losses. During negotiations, assurances were obtained from
Government that the lending rates for all loans to be made under the
project through the NHF would be at levels not less than its cost of
borrowing.



- 45 -
GDLF
4.18     The main underlying source of GDLF funds is government budgetary
allocations. Local authority requests for GDLF loans are evaluated and
included in MLGRUD's budget request. Loans by the Fund to local
authorities cover periods of 20-30 years and require semi-annual repayments
of principal and interest. The interest rate, as indicated in para. 2.37,
is tied to the cost of total government borrowings and is therefore a
floating rate adjusted periodically by the Treasury. The current rate is
9.752.
B. Proiect Impact on Local Authorities and Cost Recovery
Financial Implications
4.19     During the project period, loan proceeds from the proposed Bank
loan and Central Government would be made available to each of the
borrowers to finance its respective project s.oste. The local authorities
would bear the debt burden (principal and interest) over a period of 20
years for primary infrastructure, urban services and maintenance, and
community facilities loans, and 25 years for housing infrastructure loans,
subject to a three-year grace period. The review of the financial status
of the 21 councils that was undertaken during appraisal included an
assessment of each council's ability to afford the investments being
submitted for inclusion in the project.
4.20     The projected income and expenditures show that many of the cities
would continue to have financial surpluses after meeting their debt service
obligations on all outstanding loans as of June 1988, taking into account
loans to be received under the proposed project. Ultimately, the decision
on affordability for each council will depend upon the assumptions and
targets incorporated in the FPP. In the absence of fully developed plans,
a single indicator of affordability was tentatively adopted, namely, the
annual percentage increase in recurrent costs arising from the proposed
investment, these costs comprising the financing costs (principal and
interest) and an estimate of the additional operational cost.  A target
rate of 52 was assumed as representing an acceptable rate of growth in
recurrent expenditures. During most of the project implementation period,
many of the councils could keep within that target and some would exceed
it. During the preparation of FPPs, each council will have to decide on
the range of tariff increases considered acceptable to its community.
4.21     With regard to new housing developments under the project, by the
end of the project implementation period, the towns would have recovered
through plot sales about one-thirt of the housing infrastructure
investments made under the project. The proposed project would be
substantially self-financing and thus would not impose an additional
financial burden on the towns. On the contrary, as a result of the
strengthening of local authorities' capabilities and the transfer of long-
term financing to the private sector, the project would have a positive
impact on the local authorities' cash flow.



- 46 -
Cost Recovery
4.22     The principles of cost recovery to be applied to the various
elements of the project will be based on well established and effective
cost recovery mechanisms (see Annex 24) in place In the local authorities.
Under current practice, primary infrastructure costs are all recovered
through tariffs, rates and supplementary charges; on site residential
infrastructure costs (including roads, drainage, land purchase, site
preparation, street lighting, surveying and design costs) are recovered
through plot sales; water, and sewerage and electricity reticulation costs
through tariffs, and housing construction costs are recovered through
mortgage loan payments.
4.23     Plot prices are based on actual development costs (excluding costs
recovered through tariffs and supplementary charges) in all the project
towns. A comparison of existing plot prices in the local authorities
suggests that approximately one-third of total development costs are
currently being recovered through plot sales and the remaining two-thirds
through rates and tariffs as described above. This has proven a very
effective mechanism for full cost recovery.
4.24     Historically, the municipalities have been the financiers of low-
cost housing. In the past, they generally held ownership of the housing
themselves and rented it out at cost-covering prices. Later, however, they
shifted to a policy of selling rental stock and concentrating their
resources on the construction of low-cost housing to be sold rather than
rented. In such cases, the municipalities acted as the mortgage holders
for the sale of housing on the basis of long-term loans. Under these
arrangements the municipalities are responsible for financing both the
infrastructure and construction of housing. More recently, undar the First
Urban Project, some of the municipalities have begun to shift to
alternative private sector financing arrangements that allow them to
recover their investment capital over the short term and redeploy the
resources to meet the growing demand for housing.
4.25     Under the proposed project, this trend would be extended sector-
wide to all urban areas, with the primary responsibility for financing the
construction of housing being assumed by the private sector, and the local
authorities focusing their resources and borrowings from Central Goveranent
on expanding the supply of serviced land (see para. 2.14). Also, since
housing credit woull be made available to the beneiiciary by the private
sector (i.e., building societies), the local authorities would recover
infrastructure costs attributable to plot charges up front instead of
financing these costs over the long term. The impact of such rapid
rollover of resources available to the public sector for urban housing
would result in an increase of about 302 in investment in infrastructure
(see para. 2.15 and Annex 9 for a discussion of the impact of a revolving
fund), without additional resources beiLg required from the public sector.
4.26     The project capital development costs, excluding the pilot
regional development capital fund (Z$ 11.6 million), represent about 952 of
total project costs and all of these costs would be recovered under the
project (see Table 4.1). Approximately 202 of these costs would be
recovered directly from the beneficiaries through plot sales and most of



- 47 -
the remainder directly from the beneficiaries through existing
supplementary charges (or rates) and tariffs. Costs of community
facilities, amounting to 32 of total project costs, would be recovered
indirectly through general municipal revenues. The cost of housing
finance, which includes both house construction costs and the purchase
price of plots, will be 1002 recovered through mortgage payments. The cost
of urban services and maintenance, representing 8Z of project cost, would
be recovered partly through tariffs and partly through rates. The
remaining 82 of total project costs for regional development, and
strengthening cf institutional capacities, almost all of which is to be
provided to Government on a grant basis, would be attributed to the
government budget.

C. Building Society Operations
4.27    The building societies were established under the Building
Societies Act of 1965. Currently, there are three building societies in
Zimbabwe: (i) The Ceatral Africa Building Society (CABS); (ii) Beverly
Building Society; and (iii) Founders Building Society. Their activities
are controlled by the Registrar of the Building Societies who makes an
annual (calendar year) report to MFEPD. Tbs building societies are very
similar to British building societies, in that, for instance, they invite
savings and other deposits, for the most part short-term, from the public
and from institutions, and lend funds on a long-term basis to finance
purchase and construction of residential and commercial property.
4.28    During the past 10-year period, the building societies have grown
steadily. A 10-year (1^'76 - 1985) consolidated balance sheet of the
building societies shows that total assets more than doubled during this
period from Z$ 381 million in 1976 to Z$ 782 million in 1986. The
composition of the combined assets of the societies for 1976, 1981, and
1986 is presented in Table 4.2.

4.29     Mortgage advances and Loans constitute by far the largest element
of the asset structure. Such lending increased 882 from Z$ 298 million in
1976 to Z$ 560 million in 1986, representing around 722 of total assets in
1986. Their approved mortgage advances alone in the year ended June 30,
1988 totaled Z$ 191 million, a 31? increase over the amount in the
preceding year and 165Z over the amount in 1984. This trend suggests that
mortgage finance may continue to expand rapidly.
4.30     Under the 1965 Act, the building societies are subject to minimum
liquidity requirements. The societies are required to maintain liquid
assets equal to 15X of liabilities. Liquid assets must be maintained only
in approved deposits, or invested in government and municipal stocks with a
maturity date of not more than six years.  Approved deposits are confined
to demand or short-term deposits with government commercial banks and
discount houses. The level of liquidity maintained by the societies has
generally been above the minimum statutory requirements.
4.31     The primary sources of funds of the building societies are
deposits and shares. Of the total resources available to the societies, as
of December 1986, 612 was from deposits (fixed and savings) and 36Z from
share capital. Perhaps the most remarkable feature of the building
societies is the extent to which they have been able to raise small savings
from the public. The total number of savings accounts in the three
societies is around 700,000 --almost one account for every 10 people--
which indicates a widespread financial awareness that is exceptional in a
developing country.
4.32     Interest rates for both borrowing and lending operations are fixed
by the Treasury ir. consultation with the Registrar of Building Societies
and the building societies themselves. The building societies currently
operate a four-tier residential mortgage interest rate structure:
Loans (ZS)                    X
Up to 12,000                  12.60
12,000 - 20,000               2.176
20,000 - 40,000               13.25
Over 40,000                   13.76



- 49 -
Lending rates for mortgages on commercial properties are presently set at
14.75X. Based on an average cost of borrowing of 102 and lending
operations at 13.7?, the societies have a-l average interest rate spread of
3.7Z which is sufficient to cover administration and overhead costs, risk,
and an allowance for net profit. The experience of the building societies
with small, high volume low-income mortgages, with proportionally high
overhead costs, has been positive. Estimaces by the building societies,
based on Urban I, is that they do slightly better than break even on these
operations, and they expect to improve on this as their computerization
exercise becomes fully operational.
4.33     In the recent past, the competitive position of the building
societies was weakened by GOZ introducing tax free post office savings
bonds which competed effectively for deposits from market sources on which
the building societies have depended. These charges were based on
deliberate Government policy, in its search for additional resources in the
capital markets, to divert funds from high cost houcing. As the building
societies have shifted their lending to low income households, Government
has mudified its policies, introducing new instruments that have enabled
the building societies to compete with the post office for deposits, but
linking this mobilization of savings to levels of low income mortgage
lending by the building societies.
4.34     Ample funds therefore should be available from the building
societies to support the project. Estimates of such funds for the years
through 1994-95 are presented in Table 4.3. As indicated in the table, low
income mortgages rose from zero in previous years to 72 of the total funds
lent in 1986/87 and this percentage rose to 15? in the following year. The
trend suggests that if the percentage for low income mortgages rises to 25Z
by 1994-95 or Z$ 69.8 million, this will be sufficient to finance the
program envisaged under the project. The projected relationship between
the requirements for mortgage finance and the availability of funds for
this purpose is shown in Table 4.4. For low income housing, more than
enough funds are expected to be available during the project period, as the
building societies have committed themselves to assigning 25? of their
total lending for this purpose from the commencement of the sector program
in 1989/90 (some Z$ 17.7 million above the requirements for Urban I and the
proposed project are t.aticipated even if building society commitments of
25Z are only achieved in FY1995). In the case of moderate income housing,
the surplus above the requirement for Urban I and the proposed project is
expected to be even greater. Notwithstanding these favorable indications,
the flow of funds from the building societies to project beneficiaries will
be carefully monitored (see paras. 2.18 and 3.20).
4.35     Clearly, the proposed project greatly expands the involvement of
the building societies in the low-income housing market. The inclusion of
the building societies brings with it an important source of long-term
financing for low-income housing, and a well established and successful
mechanism for mobilizing private savings for this purpose as an alternative
to public funds.

V. ECONOMIC ANALYSIS OF THE PROJECT
A. General Approach
5.01     The principal beneficiaries of the project will be the over three
million people living in urban areas by the end of 1995, who will benefit
from improved urban services attributable to the primary infrastructure
developments and community facilities as well as the infrastructure
maintenance support under the project. The institution-building components
of the project should benefit all even larger number of people through more
efficient governmental administration, especially at the local authority
level. In addition, approximately 500,000 urban residents (with about half
estimated to be primary occupants, and half lodgers) will be provided with
improved housing; this will be nearly one fifth of the Zimbabwe urban
population (or about two thirds of new urban residents during the 1989-1995
project period).
5.02     The approach to evaluating the economic benefits and costs of the
project component relating to housing, is as follows: First, all of the
components or subcomponents related to the provision of new housing are
identified. Second. the costs of each of those items is assembled
including (i) all costs of on-site infrastructure and house construction;
(ii) the portion of the cost of off-site infrastructure financed by the
project which are attributable to the project site; (iii) costs of design
and engineering; and (iv) maintenance costs of housing and infrastructure.
Third, the annual scbedule of future investments over the project
implementation period is estimated. Fourth, the aimual schedule of housing
units to be completed is derived. Fifth, rental values per year (benefits)
are derived for the basic owner-occupiers of the housing units and for the
tenants. Finally, the ERR is calculated based on the foregoing costs and
benefits over the estimated 30-year life of the project (see Annex 25).
B. Housing and Related Infrastructure
5.03     The data underlying the calculation of the ERR for the housing and
housing-related components are shown in Table 5.1. As indicated, the ERR
is 202 for this package of investments, which comprises about 67? of the
total project. Included in the capital costs are all of the on-site
infrastructure &nd housing construction investments and 30X of the primary
infrastructure and electricity facilities investments. As indicated in
para. 5.04, the remaining 702 of the latter investments are related to
other public services. Sensitivity tests show that if total costs were 10?
higher or benefits 102 lower than projected, the estimated ERR would not be
less than 16Z.
