C. Investments in Primary Urban Infrastructure
5.04     Part of the primary infrastructure component of the project is not
directly related to housing but to commercial and industrial development
and to other public services (fire protection, hospital service, etc.).
Similarly, the project investments in community facilities (schools,
clinics, etc.) are not directly related to housing but to other public
purposes. The complex nature of the benefits to be derived from these
various investments makes it impractical to quantify them. Their
importance to the community is, of course, substantial and the need or
usefulness of the resulting urban services has been clearly established.
The project investments in facilities for non-housing purposes comprise 182
of total project cost.
D. Infrastructure Maintenance Support
5.05     The support to be given to local authorities for the improvement
of infrastructure maintenance is intended to upgrade the urban services
utilizing those basic facilities and to preserve the assets themselves.
While it is difficult to quantify the impact of the spare parts and the
replacement equipment, there is no doubt that the benefits from this
component would be substantially greater than the costs. The improved
services and service reliability should have a far reaching impact and the
prevention of premature loss of assets is clearly advantageous. This
component comprises about 102 of total project cost.
E. Assessment of Technical Assistance and Training
5.06     Institution-building is an important, though relatively low cost,
element of the project. The principal benefits from this particular
assistance will be the enhancement of technical and administrative
capabilities in the MLGRUD and indirectly in the local authorities. The
technical assistance to be provided by MLGRUD to local authorities will
lead to greater efficiency in their administration and strengthen their
capability to: evaluate development projects; (ii) practice under sound
financial management procedures; and (iii) operate and maintain urban
services with effective technical proficiency. In MLGRUD itself,
assistance will be available to enhance its capability to undertake
appraisals of proposed projects presented to the ministry by local
authorities and this support should benefit the Government in terms of
improved project design and more economic utilization of scarce investment
resources. The technical assistance under the regional development
component would support the local authorities by generating new employment
and alleviating the heavy migration to the large cities. This component
comprises less than 52 of total project costs.
F. Poverty Impact and Affordability of Housing
5.07     The rapid migration of very poor people from rural to urban areas
in recent years has resulted in heavy concentrations of the poor in cities
and they present a severe problem as they require some kind of shelter and
yet have only a limited ability to pay for even a minimal standard of
housing. Nevertheless, innovative methods of providing housing (owner-
managed house construction, provision of serviced sites by local



- 53 -
authorities and financing by building societies, redefinition of housing
standards) have brought housing within reach of many of the urban poor and
the proposed project will carry that development further.
5.08     The household poverty threshold for urban areas has been
calculated following an accepted Bank formula (poverty threshold = per
capita GNP x .35 x 1.25 x household size). On the basis of this formula,
per capita GNP of Z$ 952 and estimated household size of 5 persons, one can
derive a poverty threshold of Z$ 2,082 per year or Z$ 174 per month. Only
about 152 of the Harare high density households have incomes below Z$ 174.
The affordability calculations for the proposed project assume that the
target poor can afford to pay about 25Z of household income for housing;
accordingly, at the poverty threshold they could afford to pay 252 of
Z$ 174, or Z$ 43 per month for housing.
5.09     Household surveys conducted in the high density areas indicate
that most home-ownership units are owner occupied, with a majority of
owners renting out rooms to lodgers, usually on the basis of one family per
room rented out. Estimates based on the surveys suggest that about half
the households are lodgers and the other half home-owners. Prevailing
rents average about Z$ 40 per month per room, at or below the level
affordable to households falling under the poverty threshold.
Consequently, about half the occupants are likely to be lodgers, and all
the accommodation available to this group could be afforded by families
comprising the poorest urban households.
5.10     For the purchasers of plots under the project, it is probable that
they will be owner-occupiers and that most of them will rent out one or
more rooms. They are also likely to spend slightly more on housing-related
costs than renters because of the opportunity for equity offered by the
project; about 302 of gross income, based on earlier expenditure surveys
conducted in Zimbabwe. The building societies, which utilize 252 of gross
income as their criterion of mortgage repayment affordability, do not
include the costs of housing-related services, but do take account of
potential rental income from lodgers in determining gross income.
Consequently, the owner of a four-roomed house who rents out two rooms
would have a monthly payment obligation of Z$ 80 (Z$ 110 for the mortgage
plus Z$ 50 for services less Z$ 80 from the rental of the two rooms).
Using the assumption of willingness to spend 302 of income on housing, a
family with an income of less than Z$ 270 per month (at or below the 30th
percentile of urban income distribution in Zimbabwe) could afford to buy a
plot and build a four-roomed house.
5.11     The project would thus, in principle, provide opportunities for
shelter in which half the beneficiaries could fall below the poverty
threshold, and the remaining half would be able to afford homeownership if
they were at or below the 30th percentile of income distribution in the
urban areas. Because of widespread shortages across income groups up to
lower-middle income households, allocation criteria have been modified to
reflect shortages and unmet demand for families who cannot afford housing
currently available in the market. In terms of total demand for housing,
this project is expected to provide for approximately 702 of the
anticipated increases in the number of target (low to moderate/middle
income) households in the urban areas during the project period. The



- 54 -
incorporation of private sector financing on a sector basis is a major
factor in expanding the scale of the housing program and enables the
program to serve as a vehicle for making housing available to the urban
poor.
G. Assessment of Project Risks
5.12     One of the risks facing the project is that local authorities,
having primary responsibility for implementing most of the project, may in
some cases experience deterioration of their financial performance, thus
inhibiting their ability to effectively execute their responsibilities
under the project. This risk is mitigated by the requirement, already
initiated, to prepare and carry out FPPs in the various towns. Moreover,
technical assistance will be provided to MLGRUD under the project which
will enable the ministry to provide strengthened financial advisory
services to the local authorities. In addition, the project includes a
major training component for local authorities, which will place particular
emphasis on the financial aspects of local authority operations.
5.13     Another project risk is that the local authorities may be unable
to prepare and implement, in a timely way, capital development programs.
Their experience to date in this regard, however, has been reasonably good,
and with this function in MLGRUD and MPCNH strengthened under the project
through technical assistance, the additional support available to the
towns, should minimize the risk. Also, ample funds will be available under
the project to finance qualified consultants to assist the local
authorities in undertaking the necessary feasibility studies and
preparatory engineering design work.
5.14     A third possible risk is that the building societies will
experience limitations on their capacity to provide the necessary funds for
mortgage finance. Recent trends in the societies' ability to mobilize
savings however, has been encouraging. Moreover, in determining the
potential demand for mortgages arising out of the project, conservative
estimates of growth in building society resources were applied, well below
the actual growth demonstrated over the last three years. In addition, the
boards of all three building societies have adopted resolutions,
communicated to Government, that they will further shift their current
lending practices towards a greater share of mortgage funds being allocated
to the project (from a current level of 15Z to a minimum of 25X).



- 55 -
VI. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATIONS
6.01     During negotiations, the following agreements were confirmed:
(a) Criteria for towns to be eligible to participate under the
project (see para. 2.03);
(b) Prior Bank comment on community facilities program and
standards (see para. 2.12);
(c) Building societies to utilize satisfactory lending criteria
(para. 2.13);
(d) Establishment of a revolving fund in each local authority
participating in the housing component of the project (see
para. 2.16);
(e)  On-lending terms of Government loan to the building societies
for the financing of the computer equipment; level of
participation of the building societies; monitoring of
capacity of building societies to mobilise sufficient savings
(see para. 2.18); and progress reporting format to be
utilized by the building societies (see para. 3.20);
(f) The role and content of the FPP, and the establishment of
November 30, 1990 as target date for completion of FPPs by
the local authorities (see para. 2.25);
(g) Terms and conditions for onlending from GDLF and NHF to the
local authorities and from GOZ to ZESA and the building
societies (see para. 2.37);
(h) Overall project coordination arrangements and staffing
structure of PCMU (see para. 3.03);
(i) Recruitment of technical assistance by December 31, 1989 to
assist MLGRUD and MPCNH (see para. 3.04);
(j) Appraisal procedures and criteria (see para. 3.06);
(k) A posteriori review by the Bank of all Project Appraisal
Summaries, except for projects estimated to cost more than
US$5.0 million, for which Project Appraisal Summaries would
be sent to the Bank for prior approval (see para. 3.07);
(1) The policy of progressive development, with the acceptable
minimum initial level of provision being one room plus
ablution facility (see para. 3.10);
(m) The schedule for recruiting key PCMU staff by December 31,
1989 (see para. 3.15);



- 56 -
(n) Procurement procedures (see para. 3.28);
(o) Auditing arrangements, schedule for preparing audited
accounts, and arrangements and scheduling of semi-annual
progress reports (see para. 3.35);
(p) Annual financial targets to be met by local authorities
(see para. 4.12); and
(q) All accounts of local authorities participating in the
project be made current so that the accounts for FY89/90 can
be closed by December 31, 1990, with audits completed within
nine months thereafter (see para. 4.13).
6.02     The completion of subsidiary loan agreements, satisfactor', to the
Bank, between Government and each of the eligible local authoriti(s
participating under the project, and between Government and ZESA and each
of three building societies respectively, would be conditions of
disbursement to each implementing agency for expenditures incurred under
the project.
Recommendations
6.03     With the assurances and agreements indicated above, the project is
suitable for a Bank loan of US$80.0 million equivalent, on standard terms,
to Zimbabwe.

ZIMBABWE
URBAN SECTOR AND REGIONAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Criteria to be Met by Additional Local Authorities
to Participate in the Prolect
In addition to the 21 local authorities determined to be eligible
under the project, any of the remaining towns which qualify for funding
under the GDLF may participate subject to their demonstrating that they
possess the necessary managerial, technical and financial capacity to
successfully implement the proposed investment under the project.
Consequently, the following eligibility criteria, which each
council must satisfy, have been established:
(a)  the council must demonstrate a satisfactory staffing capacity
for managing the preparation and implementation of
infrastructure projects. This should be in the form of a
staffing plan, which could include reference to the technical
assistance and training support provided under the project.
The council's past record of performance would be taken into
account;
(b)  the council must demonstrate satisfactory organizational and
staffing arrangements for operating and maintaining its
services. The staffing plan mentioned in (a) above should
make reference to maintenance. Past performance with
maintenance would be taken into account;
(c) council must have submitted to MLGRUD a Financial Recovery
Plan which demonstrates that it will be able, during a period
not exceeding 5 years, to eliminate all of its accumulated
deficit, if any, and tn bring each main account into surplus.
The FPP must include an estimate of the financial impact of
the proposed investment and it must indicate the source of
funding of the additional recurrent costs that would be
occasioned by the proposed investment;
(d) the FPP must have been formally approved by the Council, and
endorsed by MLGRUD, and a written undertaking provided to
MLGRUD that the Plan will be implemented without modification
unless prior agreement is reached with the Ministry to change
the plan;
(e) the council must not be in arrears with its repayments to the
General Development Loan Fund or to the National Housing
Fund; and
(f) the council must demonstrate that within 12 months it will be
current in the submission of its financial accounts for
audit.
In addition, any proposed investments must satisfy the appraisal
criteria adopted by MLGRUD. Also, if the legal or administrative status of
the council is to be changed, a revised FPP will have to be prepared by the
council and submitted for approval by MLGRUD. The revised administrative
organization will not be in arrears of loan repayments and its investment
proposals must meet the appraisal criteria of MLGRUD.
Source: Bank Mission.

ZIMBABWE
URBAN SECTOR AK) REGIONAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Development Plans and Financial Status of Harare City Council
HARARE CITY COUNCIL
Mayor:           S. C. Tawengwa         Province:  Mashonaland Central
Deputy Mayors   J. W. Thembani         Phone:      791711
Town Clerk:     E. C. M. Kanengoni    Addresst  Box 990 Harare
Populations
1982:           658 364 (estimated)   1987:        961 471 (estimated)
General Description of City
Harare. the capital city of Zimbabwe has a strong industrial base.
It has since Independence witnessed unprecedented growth in all spheres and
such growth is beginning to put pressure on infrastructure and services.
In the movement of goods and people Harare is the international gateway
into the country while regionally it is the largest employer in all
sectors. Harare covers 543 square kilometers, is about 1500 m above sea
level, has 830 mm average annual rainfall and its land has a developed
property assessment value of over Z$1520 million.
The city's water is abstracted from four dams on the Hanyame
River, i.e. Prince Edward Dam and Henry Hallam Dam which supply the Prince
Edward Water Works and Lake McIlwaine and Lake Robertson which supply the
Morton Jaffray Water Works. The town of Chitungwiza is linked to Harare's
water supply network and draws a peak of about 30 MLD from the system. The
present average daily consumption (including Chitungwiza) is estimated at
320 MLD. Total treatment capacity is understood to be 477 MLD. The city
has 805 ML of sterage at present.
All the industrial and high density residential areas and about
202 of the low density area are sewered, with the remainder on septic
tanks.   Two sewage treatment works, Firle (72 MLD) and Crowborough (54
MLD) treat nearly all of the city's sewage.   Since both these works are
sited near rivers flowing into Lake McIlwaine, effluent discharge
regulations require tertiary treatment to prevent eutrophication of the
lake.   This is achieved by:
(a)   a modified activated sludge process which removes nitrates
and phosphates; and
(b)   irrigation of effluent to cattle pastures operated by the
city.
Surface water is drained by a separate piped system.
Harare has an excellent road network and some 150 traffic signal
sets of which 63 are computer controlled. The city offers a full range of
municipal services which include solid waste collection utilising in part
compaction vehicles.



- 67 -                     Annex 5
Page 2 of 16
Issues and problems
1.       The combined capacity of the Prince Edward Dam and Morton Jaffray
Water Works will be exceeded in approximately four years time. The lHarare
Water Supply Project', funded partly by the African Development Bank and
the European Investment Bank, is soon to be implemented. This will augment
the capacity of the Morton Jaffray Works and the associated pumps and
pipelines by an additional 225 Mlid. To maintain supplies at adequate
pressures at peak times in the city distribution system improvement are,
however, necessary. Also additional storage would be required to maintain
the average daily supply capacity presently enjoyed.
2.       The increasing quantity of sewerge entering both the Firle and
Crowborough Sewage Works will necessitate the construction of further
modified activated sludge (M.A.S.) treatment units at each installation in
the next five years.
3.       Over the past few years, increasing eutrophicaton has occurred in
the Henry Hallam and Prince Edward Dams. If studies confirm that this is
caused by residential areas to the south east of the city in the Henry
Hallam catchment (areas currently served by oxidation ponds and pit
latrines), a likely solution is to construct an outfall sewer to by-pass
the ponds, going down to a new M.A.S. sewage works which would also serve
the area on pit latrines (Epworth).
4.       As the city expands towards the north west, a new trunk water main
and new reservoir at Mount Hampden will be required. A new sewage works on
the Gwebi River at Selby with an associated outfall sewer will also be
required to serve development in this direction but this could likely be
deferred until beyond 1995 by developing land to the west as a first
,priority. These areas (e.g. Bluff Hill, Gillingham and Heaney) can be
served largely by existing infrastructure which is already planned for
augmentation, e.g., Crowborough STP expansion and the Little Marimba
outfall sewer duplication.
5.       The problem of acquiring spare parts for vehicles, plant and
equipment is affecting maintenance of essential infrastructure services
such as refuse collection and disposal and road maintenance just to mention
a few.
Housing
The first World Bank Urban Development project assisted in
financing a total of 4 822 ;erviced stands in Harare, ie. Glen Norah - 916
units, Sunningdale 1 449 units, Budiriro (MPG) 2 457 units. The take up
rate for the stands was retarded by financial limitations of the building
societies. Despite rent regulations, lodgers are paying unofficial fees of
between $25 - $30 per room per month. During the last three years 500
serviced stands were developed (in Kuwadzana) by employers for their
employees .

Availability of Land
Land in Municipal ownership which is immediately available for
residential development will provide 11 457 serviced plots (ie. Budiriro
Phases II - V, 9126; Kuwadzana North 1 986; Glen View 250; and Warren
Park D 95).   Planning is at an advanced stage for 300 middle/upper income
flats (funding for these flats is not yet assured). During 1988/89. parts
of Budiriro Phases II - V are planned to be implemented. In addition
Crowborough (369 stands), Warren I-ark A & B (610 stands) and Dzivaresekwa
infill (410 stands) are also planned for 1988/89. For further development,
land has to be acquired. The areas identified for acquisition are Bluff
Hill (1500 stands) and Gillingham (12500 stands).
Demand for Housing
The City has proposed a Three-Year Housing Development Plan, the
aim of which is to eliminate the housing backlog by 1990. According to
this Plan, council would be required to provide 18 000 serviced stands per
annum in order to meet demand for housing and also to eliminate the housing
backlog. In practical terms, taking into account financial constraints
this is unlikely to be implementable.
Important Note on Indicative Investment Programs
Primary Infrastructure
This Indicative Investment program for Primary Infrastructu-e has
been formatted based on priority technical need. Projects and/or annual
progress would be subject to detailed appraisal when annual PSIP
submissions are presented to MLGRUD. However the Investment Program as
outlined in this Annex is unlikely to be affordable from Government
viewpoint. Similarly, in many cases affordability of Town Councils to the
proposed levels of investment would require revenue increases that would
clearly be difficult to achieve and sustain.
For these reasons Local Authorities would be required to further
prioritize their programs when annual PSIP submissions are made in order to
achieve the requisite reductions. This would be achieved by omitting or
deferring subprojects. Overall project costs are based on this reduced
level of expenditure and not on the program as outlined in this technical
annex.



- 70-                      Annex 5
Page 5 of 16
Urban Services and Maintenance
Programs outlined for Urban Services and Maintenance would not be
required to be further reduced in view of their urgent nature and overall
project costs, based on the Urban Services and Maintenance Program included
in this Annex. However, in annual PSIP submissions it would still be
necessary to fully justify vehicle and equipment requirements.
Housing
Housing related infrastructure will also be subject to possible
further reductions during the annual PSIP process. The investment program
for servicing of stands under the sector loan program will generally not
exceed 60% of the total PSIP allocation in any given year, and approvals
for respective towns will be made accordingly by MPCNH at the time of PSIP
approval.

1.       Financial Manatement
1.1      The City Treasurer's Department employs 800 staff.  The senior
staff are well qualified with 30 accountants and 12 accounting technicians
in post. There are 40 staff in the Internal Audit Section. For many years
traditionally, the council has operated an extensive and effective training
programme. However, in the last twelve months there has been a high
turnover of staff with eight qualified accountants leaving the department.
1,2      The most recent set of audited accounts is for 1985/86.  The
1986187 accounts are in the course of being finalised.
2.       Implementation Capacity
2.1      Over the five years from 1983/84 to 1987/88 the council's capital
outlay has averaged $53.0 million per annum. The proposed investment
averages $35.0 million per annum which will be within the implementation
capacity of the council.

3.3         In the Council's 1988/89 budget it adopted stingent measures
designed to halt the decline in its financial position. Substantial
increases in tariffs and charges were introduced across a wide range of
services: rates, 38S; supplementary charges, 38S; refuse collection, 45Z;
water, 152. Expenditure was cut both for employee costs and for repairs
and maintenance. The Council's intention in 1988/89 is to halt the decline
in the Rate Account and to generate a $4.4 million surplus in the Water
Account. However, the Rate Account will require several years before its
accumulated deficit will be eliminated.

assessed as being technically feasible for implementation by the Councils

4.2         The additional recurrent costs arising from the proposed
investment were estimated and these are shown above. In 1989/90 expenditure
will increase by 2.9%, excluding the impact of inflation.
4.3         Although the increases in recurrent costs are 1lw they exclude
the existing commitment to the major water supply scheme being funded by EIB
and ADB.  Under this agreement the Council is committed to annual increases
in water charges of 5% in real terms. The additional capital investment
submitted under this project will add a further 2 to 3 percentage points to
the increased charges required each year. Depending on the 'mpact of
inflation charges may need to be increased by 152 - 201 each year until
1993194.
4.4         A major component of the programme consists of new investment in
vehicles, plant and equipment. The additional costs will fall on the Rate
Account. It is too early in the recovery process to assess the success of
the measures taken by Council to halt the decline in this account.
Therefore it is possible that the additional costs may not be affordable.
But it will require a more detailed analysis, through the preparation of a
long term recovery plan, to assess the likely impact on rates, tariffs and
charges.

ZIMBABWE
URBAN SECTOR AND REGIONAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Development Plans and Financial Status of Gweru City Council
GWERU CITY COUNCIL
Mayor        s R. Paradza                    Midlands Province
Dep Mayor   : F. Chigwira                    Phone   : 154-2151
Town Clerk  : G. Nemachena                   Address : Box 278, Gweru
Consulting engineers: John Burrow & Partners (Zimbabwe)
Population:
1982  :84,000                                1988: 100,000
General Description of Town:
Gweru has a strong industrial base and serves larger industries
such as Bata, Zimbabwe Alloys, Kariba Batteries etc. These industries are
expanding and heavy industrial sites with railway sidings are no longer
available. Gweru functions also as the Capital of Midlands Province.
The City is supplied with water from 4 dams yielding over 70,000 cu
m/day. The main water treatment plant (WTP) at Gwenoro has a nominal
capacity of 32 MLD. About 28 MLD on average is delivered to the city of
which about 302 is supplied to industry. Treated water is stored in 7
reservoirs (providing 48 hours supply capacities) at Gweru Kopje from where
it gravitates to the supply systems. All consumers are metered and a flat
rate tariff is applied. Sewage is discharged to Christmas Gift STP
(Capacity 18,200 cu m/day); to pond systems (1 to 4) which take Mkoba South
housing area, and to pond system 5 which takes Mkoba North. City roads are
generally wide, bitumenized end in good condition. Housing area .oads are
generally unpaved except for main transport routes. The city centre is
presently without a completed surface water drainage system and is prone to
flooding in heavy storms. Of the Council's fleet of about 100 vehicles for
S'WM and highway maintenance about 502 are not operational due to lack of
spare parts. Many are reaching the end of their useful lives.
Issues, Problems and Proposed Responses
Infrastructure and maintenance
1.       The Council has embarked on phase 2 of the Gwenoro WTP development
to increase capacity from 50,000 cu m/d to 60,000 cu m/d but lacks funds to
complete the phase as well as Phase 1 (40,000 cu m/day to 50,000). A
storage reservoir and parts of the 35 km A.C and steel third transmission
pipeline are completed. It would be important to complete Phases 1 and 2 as
a matter of priority as considerable investment in the scheme has already
been made and the lack of adequate supply is becoming a serious problem in
the city. Currently only about 28 MLD is able to be delivered to the city
approximating to an average of about 150 lphd assuming about 252 losses. A
third phase of the scheme (60,000 cu m/day to 75 cu m/day) has also been



- 83 -
Annex 6
Page 2 of 13
proposed but with completion of phase 2 and if consideration is given to a
conservation program (reduction of losses and progressive tariffs) adeq)ate
supplies should be available beyond 1995 and phase 3 can thus be
correspondingly deferred.
2.       Sewerage development envisages a new STP at Cambridgeshire Farm
(adjacent to Council Farm) and abandonment of remaining ponds in use at
Pondsets 1 to 4 site due to the proximity to the expanding high density
residential area. A pumping station is already constructed to discharge
sewage to pondset 5 as is a new gravity trunk sewer from pondoet 5 to the
proposed new STP site.   Feasibility studies have indicated that
pretreatment (anaerobic) ponds and biofiltration is the optimum treatment
solution for the particular site and effluent is to be used to irrigate the
Council's farm.
The Christmas Gift STP (serving a different catchment area) is at
present only about 70% utilised. Sewers in the older residential areas of
the town suffer periodic failure and an upgrading/repair program is
required.
3.       To begin to address periodic flooding problems in the CBD the
Council consider an effective stormdrainage system is necessary. Funding
has been allocated although feasibility of the scheme needs to be reviewed.
4.       The level of outshedding of vehicles is becoming critical and spare
parts and replacement vehicles are necessary it service levels (eg. refuse
collection frequency) and general city maintenance are not to deteriorate
further.
Housirni
High density hous4ng is provided in chree major areas, MKOBA SANKA
and in LOWER GWERU. About 502 of the rental housing stock has been
converted into home ownership (December 1987). A masterplan is under
preparation and UDC has been requested to assist with this. A draft report
is expected to be available September 1989. In the Mkoba area, land is
still available for about 3500 stands. Further development of high density
housing will take place in the Senka Suburb. Currently, the Building
Society CABS is constructing 551 houses with the assistance of contractors.
The remaining land areas in the Senka Suburb have not yet been acquired for
future housing development. The Senka .trea is well served with bulk
infrastructure services. Lodgers pay cirrently $30-$60 a room per month.
The council rent out shops and workshops in small commercial areas in
various suburbs.
The housing program for the years 1989194 is estimated to require
1,450 low-income stands and 490 moderate-income stands.

Remarkss
New rates became effective 1 July, 1988.   New rates 1 August, 1988.  Civil
engineering costs for a 312 sq m plot are for roads and stormwater drainage
$692, water $398, sewerage $645, towerlighting $67 and electricity $500
(July 87).
Important Note on Indicative Investment Programs
Primary Infrastructure
This Indicative Investment program for Primary Infrastructure has
been formatted based on priority technical need. Projects and/or annual
progress would be subject to detailed appraisal when annual PSIP
sthmissions are presented to MLGRUD. However the Investment Program as
outlined in this Annex is unlikely to be affordable from Government
viewpoint. Similarly, in many cases affordability of Town Councils to the
proposed levels of investment would require revenue increases that would
clearly be difficult to achieve and sustain.
For these reasons Local Authorities would be required to further
prioritize their programs when annual PSIP submissions are made in order to
achieve the requisite reductions.   This would be achieved by omitting or
deferring subprojects. Overall project costs are based on this reduced
level of expenditure and not on the program as outlined in this technical
annex.
Urban Services and Maintenance
Programs outlined for Urban Services and Maintenance would not be
required to be further reduced in view of their urgent nature and overall
project costs are based on the Urban Services and Maintenance Program
included in this Annex. However in annual PSIP submissions it would still
be necessary to fully justify vehicle and equipment requirements.
Housint
Housing related infrastructure will also be subject to possible
further reductions during the annual PSIP process. The investment program
for servicing of stands under the sector loan program will generally not
exceed 60S of the total PSIP allocation in any given year, and approvals
for respective towns will be made accordingly by MPCNH at the time of the
PSIP.

1.       Financial Manaaement
1.1      The treasury  consists  of  105  staff  and  the  senior staff are
qualified. The financial accounts are up to date and the 1986/87
accounts have been audited.
2.       Implementation Capacity
The Council spent $4.9 million on new investment in 1986/87.   The
indicative estimates of investment under the Bank project averages
$6.0 million each year over five years. The council is confident
that a programme of this size is within its implementation
capacity.
3.       Financial Status
3.1     T'he Council's financial position is weak  and becoming worse.   It
earned a deficit in 1986/87 in excess of $1.2 million which
increased the accumulated deficit at 30 June 1987 to $8.3 million.
The  position  deteriorated   further   during  1987/88 with  the
accumulated deficit reaching $9.0 million at 30 June 1988. The
deficit is analysed as follows:
$ million
Water                    (4.49)
Incorporated Areas       (3.01)
Welfare                  (3.57)
Health                   (0.70)
Refuse                   (0.19)
Rates                     2.30
Other                     0.66
(9.00)
3.2      In 1988/89 the Council has  implemented substantial increases in a
wide range of charges in order to halt the financial decline.
However the Council continues to be arrears with its repayments of
loans to Government. The arrears are in excess of $3.0 million.

4.3      The proposed investment will  impose  a considerable burden on the
Council  at  a  time  when   it will  need  to  increase  charges
substantially as part  of  its  financial  recovery  plan.   It is
questionable whether the Council will be able to afford these
extra costs given its need to pay the arrears of loans to
Government and to eliminate a $9.0 million deficit.
4.4      The Council is revising its  financial recovery plan following the
recent appraisal and, in doing so, priorities will need to be
reconsidered in order to match spending with revenues.

ZIMBABWE
URBAN SECTOR AND REGIONAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Development Plans and Financial Status of Bindura Town Board
BINDURA TOWN COUNCIL
Chairman Rural Council    : T.G. Cotnes       Province: Mashonaland Central
Chariman Townboard         : E. Chikerema    Phone   : 6952/6430
Executive off              : A.N. Makoni      Address : Box 15
Consulting engineers: Stewart & Scott (water), Interconsult (sewage)
Popvlation   1982:  22,000                    1987:   26,000 (estimated)
General Description of Town:
Biat.ura functions as the capital of the province of Mashonaland
Central. While the emphasis of the development of the town has been on the
nearby Nickel Mine, during recent years agricultural support activities
have now become well established. The Cotton Marketing Board has provided
a Gin. CMD and DDF have established depots in Bindura. Bindura is managed
by a Town Board with officials serving both Rural Council and the Town
Board. Consideration is being given to Bindura Rural Council becoming a
Municipality. Industrial land served by railways is available.
Potable water is supplied from a well maintained WTP of 9120 cu.m
per day (24 hours) capacity sited near the Mazowe river where raw water is
abstracted via a dam. Water is pumped to storage reservoirs (10,000 cu m
capacity) from where it gravitates to serve the town via three transmission
pipelines of 375 mm, 250 mm and 150 mnm diameters. Wastewater is disposed
of either by septic tanks in low density and industrial areas with middle
and high density areas to the north and south of the town served by
oxidation ponds. The ponds in the South have recently been constructed and
effluent is discharged to farms for irrigation. Garbage collection and
disposal and road maintenance functions are also carried out by the
Council.
Issues, Problems and Proposed Responses
Infrastructure and maintenance:
1.       Whereas treatment and main storage of water appear adequate for
the near term, reticulation has been carried out in an ad-hoc manner and
the system could be rationalised giving greater efficiency.
2.       Proposed housing development at Wild Dog Valley will necessitate
extension to sewage pond arrangements and, in the longer term, construction
of new ponds to the south east. The development will necessitate the
acquisition of some 195 hectares of land in total (20ha for roads, 175ha
for Chiwaridza phase II). For phase III (between phase II and roads) a
further 225ha would be required. It would be important to commence
acquisition procedures as soon as possible if implementation of the housing
i nnt tnf  ii 1^V 



- 96 -                     Annex 7
Page 2 of 12
3.       The Council carries out most of its own maintenance on its
vehicles and plant, about 35 items, at its 2 workshops. Additional
equipment at workshops would assist in maintenance activities.
4.       At present the Council fire engine is non-operational.
Housinz
As a result of the decentralisation of Central Government services
there is a high demand for rental houses. Lodgers pay at the moment
Z$12/month per room. The immediate supply of high density housing will
take place in a new area, Wild Dog Valley which has already been acquired.
A new township for this area, Chiwaridzo, has been planned by Physical
Planning which provides 65 medium density plots, and 600 high density
plots. Future development is expected to take place south of Chivaridzo.
USAID has earmarked finances to service 300 high density plots in the
Chiwaridzo Area, during 1987-89. The remaining plots in the Chiwaridzo
area have been allocated for private development. Plots for the 1989/90
programme are planned to be located on the area south of Chiwaridzo area
which is still to be acquired. The HLGL has been informed about thc land
acquisition programme and negotiations with the land owners for 425ha
should start shortly.

Important Note on Indicative Investment Protrams
Primary Infrastructure
This Indicative Investment program for PrImary Infrastructure has
been formatted based on priority technical need. Projects and/or annual
progress would be subject to detailed appraisal when annual PSIP
submissions are presented to MLGRUD. However the Investment Program as
outlined in this Annex is unlikely to be affordable from Government
viewpoint. Similarly, in many cases affordability of Town Councils to the
proposed levels of investment would require revenue increases that would
clearly be difficult to achieve and sustain.
For these reasons Local Authorities would be required to further
prioritize their programs when annual PSIP submissions are made in order to
achieve the requisite reductions.   This would be achieved by omitting or
deferring stsprojects. Overall project costs are based on this reduced
level of expenditure and not on the orogram as outlined in this technical
annex.
Urban Services and Maintenance
Programs outlined for Urban Services rnd Maintenance would not be
required to be further reduced in view of their urgent nature and overall
project costs, based on the Urban Services and Maintenance Program included
in this Annex. However in annual PSIP submissions it would still be
necessary to fully justify vehicle and equipment requirements.
Housing
Housing related infrastructure will also be subject to possible
further reductions during the annual PSIP process. The investment program
for servicing of stands under the sector loan program will generally not
exceed 60X of the total PSIP allocation in any given year, and approvals
for respective towns will be made accordingly by MPCNH at the time of PSIP
approval.

1.       Financial Management
1.1      The Treasury  consists  of  eight  staff  headed  by the Assistant
Treasurer who is not professionally qualified and who graduated from
university in 1986.   There  is  one  internal  auditor who reports to the
Chairman of the Council.



- 98 -                        Annex 7
Page 4 of 12
1.2      The accounts are up  to  date.    The 1986/87 financial statements
have been audited and the certificate issued without qualification.
2.       Implementation Capacity
2.1      In  1986/87  the   council's   capital  outlay  totalled  $294,000
(excluding the Welfare Management  Account).    The  program for the Bank's
project averages $700 000 per year over five years. This size of program
should not impose a significant burden upon the technical and managerial
capacity of the council's staff.
3.       Financial Status
3.1      Although  incurring  a  small  deficit,  the  counc.l's  financial
performance improved significantly during 1986/87. This reversal of recent
poor results was due mainly to a substantial rise in charges during the
years
50% increase in water charges
1002 increase in service charges
over 50% increase in rates
3.2      The Council's position  improved  further  during 1987/88 with its
accumulated deficit of $313 000 being reduced to $157 000:
Rural Roads            ($ 448.0)
Housing                ($ 476.6)
Designated Areas        $ 199,1
Welfare Managemen-      $ 568.5
($ 157.0)
3.3      The Council's draft  financial  recovery  plan  estimates that the
overall deficit will be removed during 1988/89 and the individual deficits
on Rural Roads and Housing eliminated  by  30  June 1992.   The Council has
agreed to implement increased charges for water, services and rural roads.
4.       Affordability of Proposed Investment
4.1      During Appraisal the following investment programme was assessed as
being technically feasible for the Council to implement:

ZIMBABWE
URBAN SECTOR AND REGIONAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Housing Revolving Fund to be Operated by Local Councils
Introduction
1.       It is intended that under this project individual local
authorities will borrow from the National Housing Fund (NHF) to fund the
construction of serviced plots. These loans will be repaid in accordance
vith prevailing terms and conditions. Currently, loans are repaid to the
NHF on an annuity basis at 9.75Z over 30 years.
Obiective
2.       The purpose of establishing a Revolving Fund within a local
authority is to provide a mechanism whereby receipts from the sale of
serviced stands (constructed under the project) are available immediately
to fund new construction. The creation of the fund is intended also to
secure the repayment of the original loan from Government.
Description
3.       A separate Fund will be established within each council's
financial accounts. Amounts advanced from the NHF under this project will
be credited to the Fund on their receipt.
4.       Advances will be made to the borrowing accounts (housing, roads,
water and sewerage) to fund the cost of construction. The borrowing
accounts will repay the loan (principal and interest) to the Fund on the
same terms and conditions required by the NHF.
5.       When serviced stands, constructed with advances from the Revo'ving
Fund, are sold and a capital receipt obtained, the proceeds will be
credited to the Fund. They will add to the balance available for further
advances to borrowing accounts and the service debt. The borrowing
accounts will repay these advances/loans on the same terms and conditions
which apply to the NHF.
Requirements in Establishing and Operating a Fund
6.       The accounting for the Fund's transactions will be separate from
the council's other funds. The Fund will be subject to the annual audit of
the council's external auditor. The format of the Fund's Income and
Expenditure Account is shown on page two of this Annex.
7.       Advances from the Fund will be used solely for the purposes of
constructing serviced stands in accordance with the specifications set out
in the Loan Agreement between the Bank and Government.
8.       Annual provisions will be made in the Fund for the complete ani
prompt repayments of loans (principal and interest) to the NHF.
9.       Surplus balances on the Fund will be invested until required for
further advances to borrowing accounts. Interest earned on these



- 111 -                      Annex 9
Page 2 of 2
investments will be credited to the Fund. In the absence of specific
external investments, any balance held on the Fund will attract interest at
an average rate consistent with that obtainable externally. This
'notional' interest will be credited to the Fund and charged to the Rate
Account.

ZIMBABWE
URBAN SECTOR AND REGIONAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Requirements for Preparation of Financial Performance Plans
The Financial Performance Plan (FPP) to be prepared by each Local
Authority will be organized in accordance with the following headings and
schedules, each of which is briefly explained. An illustration of the
format is provided following the introducticn.
Summary of Actions
Statement of actions to be taken by (a) Local Authority (b) MLGRUD
(c) any other party, the date of implementation of these actions and the
estimated financial impact of these actions.
Current Financial Position
Statement of opening accumulated surplus or deficit analysed by
account. Description of the 3-5 year trend in the Local Authority's
financial performance including comments on its ability to repay loans and
on the availability of reserves or balances. Statement of current position
in posting financial accounts and certification by external auditors.
Explanation of factors leading to deterioration of financial
position. Indication of significant issues likely to affect future
performance including, for example, additional services or reoreanisation of
local administration.
Financial Performance Plan
Description of the main components of the FPP.
Assumption Risks and Constraints
The principal assumptions underlying the FPP need to be stated
including, for example, the inflation rate adopted, the borrowing approvals
required or the population growth. The major risks or constraints on the
success of the FPP should be listed.
Action Required (Cost Reduction and Income Maximization)
The main actions required will be explained for (a) the Local
Authority as a whole and (b) the individual main service accounts. These
actions will distinguish between those measures which are intended to reduce
costs and those measures intended to maximise income. For each of the
actions an estimate of the financial impact will be presented showing the
additional revenue and/or reduced expenditure in each of the years covered
by the FPP.



- 114 -              Annex 11
Page 2 of 6
Targets (Financial and Manpower)
Targets will be listed against which the FPP performance will be
monitored by the Local Authority and by MLGRUD. These targets will be
mainly financial although manpower and efficieuLcy targets may be included.
The financial, performance targets will be expressed in terms of total
expenditure, total income. total employee costs, total running costs or
total debt charges. The targets will be listed on an annual basis over the
FPP period.
Responsibilities and Implementat.on Schedule
For each of the actions required in the FPP, responsibility for
their implementation will be specified and target dates agr od. The
responsibilities primarily will rest with the Local Author :y, but the
relevant Ministry may have a crucial role to play.
Schedule A - Summary Analysis of A,nmual Surplus/Deficit by Account over .he
FPP Period
This schedule presents a summary, by main bervice account, of the
estimated year end surplus/deficit over the FPP period. The schedule will
show the years in which individual accounts begin to achieve surpluses and
to eliminate their accumulated deficits and, also, when the Local Authority,
in overall terms, generates surpluses.
Schedule B - Detailed Analysis of Annual Surplus/Deficit by Account over the
FPP Period
This schedule presents the detailed analysis by main service
account from which Schedule A is derived. For each account total
expenditure is analysed across three principal headings of 'employee costs',
'operational costs' and 'debt charges'. The income for each account is
shown in total and the resulting surplus or deficit is presented.
Schedule C - Capital Investment Program : Payments over the FPP Period
This schedule presents the capital program upon which the FPP has
been based. For each of the years, it will show the capital payments
analysed across the main service accounts including, for example, water
supply, sewerage, roads and drainage and housing.
Schedule D - Capital Investment Program : Recurrent Costs over the FPP
Period
This schedule includes the estimated recurrent costs arising from
the capital program included in Schedule C.   The costs are analysed by main
service account across each of the years in the FPP period.
Schedule E - Impact on Rates, Charges, Fees, and Tariffs over FPP Period
This schedule will contain detailed illustrations of the tariffs to be
charged for each main service account in each year of the FPP.
Source: Bank Mission

BINDURA RURAL COUNCIL
FINANCIAL PERFORMANCE PLAN OVER FIVE YEARS 1989/90 TO 1993/94
SUMMARY OF DATED ACTIONS TO BE TAXEN
1.       Bindura Council's Financial Performance Plan (FPP) has been
designed to recover the accumulated deficits on all relevant accounts by 1
July 1994.
2.       The principal actions required to implement the FPP are as
follows:-
(a)  to increase the unit tax in the Rural Roads Account from $300
per unit to:
$ 700 by no later than 1 July 1989
$ 750 by no later than 1 July 1990
$ 860 by no later than 1 July 1991
$ 980 by no later than 1 July 1992
$1100 by no later than I July 1993
(b)  to increase water charges by 1OZ per annum by no later than 1
July each year; and
(c) to increase service charges by 102 per annum by no later than
1 July each year; and



- 120 -                   Annex 12
Page 2 of 10
(d) to restrict increases in expenditure to the increases arising
from inflation and from the effects of the approved
investment programme. The Council's manpower budget will not
exceed XX during the FPP period.
CURRENT FINANCIAL POSITION
3.       The expected accumulated surplus/(deficit) on each of the main
accounts is as follows:-
Z$o00
1 July 1987    1 July 1988    1 July 1989
Designated Areas     25.5           199.1           551.7
Rural Roads         (238.4)        (448.0)         (220.3)
Housing             (511.8)        (476.6)         (248.7)
Welfare Management  411.3           568.5           727.2
Overall Surplus/
(deficit)       (313.4)         (157.0)         809.9
4.       The accounts requiring recovery actions are Rural Roads and
Housing. During 1987/88 the financial performance of the Roads Account
continued to decline with an annual deficit of $209,600, compared with an
original estimated surplus of $108,000. The Housing Account generated a
small surplus of $35,200 which marked a reversal of the previous trend of
deficits. However, actual performance did not match the estimated surplus
of $67,000.
5.       The financial performance of all accounts is expected to improve
significantly during 1988189 for the following reasons:-
(a) proposed increases in water and service charges;
(b) additional income to the Rural Roads Account arising from
capital works to be carried out on the Chiwaridzo scheme by
the Council's labour force; and
(c) additional income arising from the construction of 800 stands,
most of which is being funded by USAID.
6.       The financial accounts of the Council are up to date and the
Treasurer's Department is fully staffed to the approved establishment
level.
PERFORMANCE PLAN
Assumptions, Risks and Constraints
7.       The FPP has been based on the 1988/89 estimates.  Expenditure in
later years has been inflated by 1OZ per annum excluding debt charges.
8.       It is assumed that borrowing approval will be given for the
capital investment programme detailed in Schedule C attached. For the

10.      The legal status of the Council remains unchanged throughout the

GVERU CITY COUNCIL
FINANCIAL PERFORMANCE PLAN OVER FOUR YEARS 1989190 TO 1992/93
SUNMARY OF DATED ACTIONS TO BE TAKEN
1.       Gweru City Council's Financial Performance Plan (FPP) has been
designed to evaluate the accumulated deficits on all accounts by 30 June
1993.
2.       The actions required to achieve this objective are summarisied as
follows:
(a) rents, rates, fees, charges and tariffs will be increased
annually on 1 July in accordance with Schedule E to this FPP.
Note: All $ amounts in this Annex are Zimbabwe dollars.



-130-                        Annex 13
Page 2 of 22
Should inflation rates differ from those assumed in the FPP. the necessary
revision in charges will be modified accordingly;
(b) manpower numbers will be constrained to the current total
number of 1689;
(c) recurrent expenditure will increase annually only to reflect
the impact of inflation and the agreed investment programme
oet out in Schedule C.
CURRENT FINANCIAL POSITION
3.      The Council's financial position has deteriorated significantly
over the last five years since 1983184. Annual deficits have increased the
Council's accumulated deficit to $9.0 million at 1 July 1988:
Accumulated Surplus/(Deficit) $000 at
1 July 1987     1 July 1988
Rates                               1343             2300
Water                              (3647)           (4490)
Incorporated Areas                 (2887)           (3015)
Welfare                            (2722)           (3571)
Health                                -              (702)
Beer Fund                             -               637
Other                               (387)            (177)
(8300)          (9018)
4.       The accounts with the largest deficita are Water, Welfare and
Incorporated Areas. The Water account's poor: financial performance is
mainly due-to the capital investment on the augmentation scheme and the
resulting heavy incidence of capital costs. Tariffs have not been
increased in line with rising and additional costs. Similarly for the
Incorporated Areas charges are set at a level which is inadequate to
recover costs from the house occupier.
5.       The deficit on the Welfare account has increased beyond the
ability of the Beer Fund to subvent the total costs. The increasing demand
for the services and the limited opportunity to levy charges to achieve
full cost perforDasnce have increased the burden on the Rate Account to
provide the necessary working capital.
6.       In the 1988/89 Estimates the Council has budgeted to halt the
annual decline in its financial performuAnce. A large number of tariff
increases have been approved which should enable the Council, in total, to
recover costs. However, during 1988/89 no progress is expected to be made
in reducing the accumulated deficit on any account.
7.       The Financial accounts are up to date and the 1987/88 final
accounts are awaiting audit by the Urban Development Corporation.



131                        Annex 13
Page 3 of 22
PERFORMANCE PLAN
Asumpt3ons, Risks and Constraints
8.         The four year FPP has been based on the 1988189 Estimates.
Costs, other than debt charges, have been inflated by lO per annum over
the plan period. Debt charges have been calculatee on the basis of
existing commitments plus the capital programme set out in Schedule C.
Loan repayments and interests costs have been calculated on the existing
terms and conditions applied by the GDL? and NEP, namely 9.75Z over varying
periods ranging from 5 to 30 years.
9.         It is assumed that there vill be no significant changes to the
functions and responsibilities of the Council which might affect its
spending levels or its sources of revenue.
10.        The FPP is dependent upon a regular, annual. increase in tariffs
and charges. It is assumed that these will be implemented in full on the
due date.
11.        Expenditure is forecast to increase only in line with inflation
and with the additional costs arising from the capital programme. The
demand for increased services (arising from population growth) will be met
either from additional revenues -r from a redeployment of exisitng
resources resulting from changed priorities or improvements in efficiency.
Actions Required
12.      The Council will prepare a programme for the review of each of its
operations in order to ideutify opportunities for reductions in costs and
improvements in efficiency. This programme is intended to enable the
Council to maintain its overall manpower levels to 1689 or lower and to meet
increased demand without recourse to additional expenditure over the FPP
period.
13.      The Council will implement, with effect from 1 July 1989/90 and the
succeeding 3 years, the tariff increases detailed in Schedule E to this FPP.
The estimated additional income arising from these increases is set out in
the detailed financial forecasts presented in Schedule B.
Targets
14.      Total manpower numbers will not exceed 1689 during the FPP period.
15.      The Council's overall deficit will te eliminated by 30 June 1992.

Page 4 of 22

datess

Welfare:                  30 June 1993
Health:                   30 June 1993
Education:                30 June 1991
Estates:                  30 June 1993
17.      All remaining accounts will be budgeted to breakeven or to
generate a surplus each year of the FPP period.

Annex 15
- 152 -                           Page 1 of 1
ZIMBABWE
URBAN SECTOR AND REGIONAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Studies of Municipal Finance to be Undertaken Under the Project
In April 1986 the Commission of Inquiry into taxation reported on
its findings regarding the fiscal system of Zimbabwe. Recommendations were
made with regard to a number of issues affecting the funding and financial
management of local government and the relationship between central and
local government.
The MLGRUD wishes to commission a number of studies to examine
more closely the implications of several of the issues raised by the
report. The studies include the followings-
(a) the provision of free health services by local authorities
and the basis of cost recovery;
(b) the operation of commercial type enterprises as a source of
revenue;
(c) the levying of flat rates on all lands within urban centres;
(d) the exemption from rates of Government owned property;
(e) the sharing of revenue between central and local government;
(f) the funding of road maintenance carried out by local
authorities;
(g) the standardization and simplification of local authority
accounting;
(h) the operation of a deficiency grant/resource equalisation
grant;
(i) the provision of local government statistics.
The MLGRUD envisages a 3 year research programme staffed by local
academics and practitioners under a coordinator appointed by the Ministry.
The first of the studies will commence in early 1989.
Source Bank Mission.

ZIMBABWE
URBAN SECTOR AND REGIONAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
General Appraisal Procedures for Approval of
Primary Infrastructure Projects under the PSIP
1.       Council would submit to MLGRUD any project proposal (with
annexures) for PSIP consideration, as in the past. Submissions would
include in addition, the following feasibility study (level of detail will
depend on size of project); preliminary engineering designs and drawings;
detailed cost estimates; financial recovery plan.
2.       Submissions would be appraised by MLGRUD.  The g'neral criteria to
be considered in the appraisal are set out in Annex 18.
3.       MLGRUD would prepare a brief Project Appraisal Summary report
(which would include amended project detail as may be highlighted by the
appraisal) and submit this with the original PSIP submission and annexures
to the MFEPD for review.
4.       MLGRUD would copy this to MPCNH for information regarding the
availability of primary urban infrastructure to service any proposed new
housing development.
5.       MFEPD would review and inform MLGRUD that the project is approved
or disapproved for inclusion in PSIP.
6.       MLGRUD would inform council of MFEPD action.   If project is
agreed for inclusion in PSIP then council would arrange for detailed
engineering and urdate of cost estimates if necessary.   (If costs increase
by more than 151 from the original submission, PSIP would require
resubmission in accordance with 1 above with full explanation.)
7.       Council would submit request for borrowing powrers on standard form
and submit, together with detailed engineering design, drawings, revised
Financial Recovery Plan, documents and updated cost estimates, for
reappraisal by MLGRUD. Detailed technical, financial and economic
appraisal will follow the relevant sector appraisal guidelines.
8.       MLGRUD would reappraise and, if approved, would prepare a revised
Project Appraisal Summary and would communicate its approval to council
(with conditions and/or amendments as necessary).
9.       Council would implement project.
Source: Bank Mission.



- 155 -                    Annex 17
Page 2 of 2
General Appraisal Procedures for Approval
of Housing Prolects Under the PSIP
1.       Council would submit to MPCNH any housing proposal (with
annexures) for PSIP consideration, as in the past. Submissions would
include a feasibility study according to the format set out in Annex 20.
Indicative cost estimates would be required but preliminary layout and
engineering designs would be optional.
2.       Submissions would be appraised by MPCNH, primarily based on
assessment of urgency of need, capacity to prepare and implement the
proposed project, and status of preparation.
3.       MPCNH would prepare a brief Project Appraisal Summary of each
submission (which will include amended project detail as may be highlighted
by the appraisal) and submit this with the original PSIP submission and
annexures to the MFEPD for review.
4.       MPCNH would copy this to MLGRUD for information to be included in
their evaluation of the FRPP.
5.       MFEPD would review and inform MPCNH that the projects submitted by
MPCNH on behalf of the local authorities are approved or disapproved for
inclusion in the PSIP.
6.       MPCNH would inform councils of MFEPD actions.  Councils would then
arrange for preliminary and detailed engineering designs and updated cost
estimates for those projects approved for inclusion in the PSIP.
7.       Councils would submit detailed information and documentation
(including detailed engineering designs and drawings) to MPCNH together
with a copy of their request for borrowing powers. The original request
for borrowing powers would be sent concurrently by the council, together
with its updated FRPP, to MLGRUD.
8.       After a detailed review, MPCNH would prepare a revised Project
Appraisal Summary and would communicate its decision to the council and to
MLGRUD.
9.       After receiving MPCNH approval, MLGRUD would approve or disapprove
the borrowing powers request, which it would evaluate in the context of the
FRPP, and communicate its decision to MPCNH and the council.
10.      Council would implement the project.
