Page 1 of 21
ZIMBABWE
URBAN SECTOR AND REGIONAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
General Criteria for Selection and Appraisal
of SubproJects Under Parts A and B of the Proiect
A.       Introduction
1.       Local Authority proposals for projects in the PSIP would continue
to be reviewed by MLGRUD. MPCNH, and Ministry of Finance, Economic Planning
and Development (MFEPD), in accordance with the requirements of and
following the format set out in the MFEPD Circulars that are issued
annually for this purpose. Thus generally the submissions would indicate
that:
(a) all projects or sub-projects are consistent with the overall
policies and objectives of the 5 year National Development
Plan;
(b) the proposed projects or sub-projects are of the highest
priority.
2.       Although the PSIP process and procedures should remain essentially
unchanged, it is suggested that the process and techniques for appraisal of
sub-projects be strengthened to ensure that they are:
(a) technically, financially and economically viable;
(b) implementable in the time frame proposed;
(c) compatible with the overall development plans and objectives
of the town and with related sectors;
(d) the most cost efficient solutions (in terms of both capital
and recurrent costs) for dealing with the problems they are
addressing;
(e) consistent with conservation needs and the need to protect
the environment;
(f) cognizant of the institutional capacity of the Local
Authority to implement proposed projects;
(g) affordable to the Local Authority as measured by its ability
to meet the additional capital repayment and recurrent cost
obligations arising from the proposed investment, and
reflected in both the Local Authority's Financial Performance
Plan and the financial performance targets established for
the relevant service/account.
3.       It is suggested that a particularly rigorous appraisal be carried
out for projects of relatively large size.
4.       Suggested criteria for sub-project selection are shown below.
Detailed appraisal guidelines, set out in the Attachment to this Annex,



- 157 -                     Annex 18
Page 2 of 21
B.       Financial and Economic Selection Criteria for Projects
1.       Criteria applied to the financial and economic appraisal of sub-
projects will include the following:
(a)  Proposed investments must generate at least as much new
income as additional expenditures, including related
recurrent costs incurred. The following rates of return must
be demonstrated:
(i) For revenue-earning organisations. a financial rate of
return at least equal to the cost of borrowing funds and
an economic return of at least 122, except that for
certain mun3cipal operations such as water supply and
sewerage, only a part of the benefits are quantifiable
and economic rates of return of around 42 would be
acceptable.
(ii) For non-revenue earning organisations, no financial rate
of return will be calculated and an economic rate of
return will be determined only where it is possible to
quantify project benefits, in which case the return
should be at least 122 assuming all benefits are
quantified.
(b)  Proposed investments should be integrated with the Local
Authority's Financial Performance Plan, as approved by
HLGRUD. It must be demonstrated that the proposed investment
is affordable both to the Local Authority and to the
beneficiaries/ratepayers. The additional recurrent costs
arising from the investment must be quantified and the source
of funding specified.
C.       Technical
1.       Technical appraisal of sub-projects should be carried out
following the detailed guidelines provided in the Attachment. This
appraisal would review the following (PSIP submissions must include
relevant information to enable such review to be undertaken):
(a)  Subsector project implementation performance under the PSIP
in previous years including cost overruns/underruns;
effectiveness of subsector in meeting objectives (eg.,
improvement in service levels, standards, etc., such as per
capita level of water supplied; reduction in losses;
additional properties sewered; road sizes, additional length
of road provided). This performance would be measured
against service level norms and standards to be developed by
MLGRUD and MPCNH.
(b)  Expected impact of proposed subprojects on service levels,
consequences of deferring the investment, etc.



Annex 18
- 158 -                    Page 3 of 21
(c) The relationship of the subprojects to (i) the development
plan for the Local Authority; (ii) other sectors (eg. water
augmentation would normally mean attendant improvements in
wastewater collection and treatment); and (iii) housing and
industrial development plans.
D.       Institutional
The criteria to be applied in the institutional appraisal of sub-
projects will include the following:
(a) the Local Authority's previous experience of successfully
implementing subprojects of a similar size and technical
complexity;
(b) the availability of sufficient technical and managerial
resources with proven skills and ability;
(c) the existence of the procedures and controls within the Local
Authority which are necessary for the efficient and effective
planning, implementation and reporting of subproject related
activities and resulting costs.



- 159 -                           Annex 18
Page 4 of 21
Attachment
ZIMBABWE
URBAN SECTOR AND REGIONAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Detailed Guidelines to Appraisal Criteria
1.       INTRODUCTION
The purpose of this attachment is to set out detailed guidelines
for the selection of primary infrastructure or housing projects or
subprojects in Local Authorities and the subsequent comprehensive
independent appraisal of the subproject proposed. The note commences with
a list of likely eligible subsectors that would be financed under the
Project, general sector development objectives and then sets out specific
guidelines for subproject appraisal.
2.       ELIGIBLE SUBSECTORS
Investments in urban infrastructure, services and maintenance,
together with low cost housing plot development would be the broad sectors
that would be financed under the Project. The following types of
infrastructure are illustrative of the subproject components that would be
financed:
(a)  water supply, including the repair and rehabilitation of
existing plant, equipment, distribution networks as well as
extensions to cope with growth;
Cb)  wastewater collection and disposal, including the
augmentation and improvement to sewerage systems and
appropriate low cost disposal facilities;
Cc)  solid waste management, including the provision or
rehabilitation of vehicles, equipment and containers for
collection and disposal;
(d)  roads and drainage, in particular the rehabilitation and/or
upgrading of existing road and drainage networks and the
construction of new road linkages and other measures to aid
traffic flow, reduce congestion, and improve public
transportation services;
(e)  street and area lighting to enhance safety and security;
(f)  markets and public transport terminals, including the
rehabilitation of existing facilities;
(g)  comprehensive infrastructure all to appropriate standards of
new high density housing areas;
(h)  other high-priority infrastructure investments which would
improve the efficiency of Local Authorities, enhance
industrial and commercial activity and improve living
conditions in high density residential areas; and
(i)  vehicles and equipment, including spare parts and workshop
improvements as necessary to ensure Local Authorities can
provide and maintain services which they are bound to provide.



- 160 -                        Annex I8
Page 5 of 21
Attachment
In addition to physical works as outlined above, the Project could finance:
(i)    studies aimed at institutional development, enhanced resource
mobilisation and improved technical and financial management;
(ii)    technical assistance for subproject appraisal, implementation
and monitoring; and
(iii)    training.
3.       SELECTION CRITERIA
Local Authorities will be responsible for identification and
selection of particular subprojects which will need to be sanctioned by the
Borrower. The subproject should be selected in accordance with the
following criteria:
(a) economic and financial feasibility
(b) technical feasibility
(c) effective demand for services and willingness of consumers to
pay, directly or indirectly, for the services provided;
(d) feasibility of the cost recovery mechanism (s);
(e) institutional capability of the local Authority to prepare
and execute the project;
(f) the availability of technical assistance from domestic or
international sources to supplement the Local Authority's
implementation capabilities, where necessary; and
(g)  creditworthiness, including the ability of the Local
Authority to carry the additional debt imposed by the project
to be financed.
A project selected for a revenue - earning service should have a
financial rate of return equal to at least the cost of borrowing funds and
an economic return of at least 12Z, except that for certain municipal
operations such as water supply and sewerage, only a part of the benefits
are quantifiable and accordingly economic rates of return of 4Z are
acceptable. A project selected for a non-income earning service will have
no financial rate of return; an economic rate of return, however, should be
determined where it is possible to quantify project benefits and in such a
case the economic return should be at least 12? assuming all benefits use
quantified.
The technical economic and financial feasibility may be determined
on the basis of preliminary engineering and studies. A more definitive
evaluation should subsequently be considered on the basis of detailed
engineering.



- 161 -                      4nnex 18
Page 6 of 21
Attachment
4.            INTEGRATED APPROACH TO INFRASTRUCTURE PROVISION
Projects included would need to demonstrate an integrated and
balanced approach to the improvement of infrastructure and urban services.
i.e., projects which coordinate town-wide improvements in sectors and
subsectors in a logical sequence (see section). However, consideration may
be given to significant projects which contribute to the efficient
functioning of productive urban economic activity, e.g., central business
districts, markets, and industrial areas. Special consideration may be
given to urban upgrading projects which integrate the provision of
infrastructure and urban services within a particularly defined urban area
which houses a significant percentage of the urban poor.
S.       ECONOMIC, FINANC'AL AND INSTITUTIONAL APPRAISAL
(a) The economic and financial viability of the various
components of the Project must be evaluated. However, there
will usually be a need for further analysis and
prioritization. Because of the scarcity of resources, the
Economic Rate of Return (ERR) is an important basis of
evaluation. Sometimes, even with high economic rates of
return, projects may need to be postponed as unaffordable by
the Local Authority.
(b) In identifying and quantifying a project's costs and
benefits. where the economic benefits cannot be directly
estimated, the Local Authority should use projected revenues
and, in general, focus on whether demand projections and
tariffs are reasonable and whether the proposed project is
the least cost alternative. Where appropriate, avoided costs
(e.g. reduced vehicle operating costs) should be used in
determining project benefits. Certain components such as
sanitation will exhibit no meaningful prices for determining
costs and in such cases the analysis should concentrate on
appropriate service levels provided in the most cost-
effective way.
(c) The economic analysis should include foreign exchange and
employment effects.
(d) Appraisal should include an assessment of a projects's
viability in case of changes in key parameters, e.g. a 202
increase in costs or decrease in benefits, together with a
judgement on the likely variation from the basic assumptiLns.
(e) Where a project shows marginal returns or high risks, an
analysis of the risks should be done and recommendations
should be made on how to reduce risks.
(f) Where there are competing projects showing high rates of
return, preference should be given in project selection and
design to Local Authorities which are important production
and employment centers and to service centers for future
regional economic development. Local Authorities with



- 162 -                     Annex 18
Page 7 of 21
Attachment
effective local government institutions capable of
maintaining infrastructure and urban services. and of levying
and collecting suitable taxes, fees and other charges, should
be given the highest priority.
(g) Real demand for services should receive careful attention
because of the extreme scarcity of resources at this time.
However, any existing disparties in service levels among
Local Authorities and regions should not be inadvertently
widened and the urban poor should receive significant
benefits from projects financed under the Project.
(h) The ability of a prospective borrower to recover investment
costs through plot sales, user charges, or through increases
in property taxes and other forms of local revenue
enhancement will probably be the critical determinant in the
project selection.
(i) Tne rehabilitation and maintenance of existing infrastructure
is expected to take priority over new investments in most
cases.
(j) Institutional development is expected to be an important
component in the project. In many cases this will initially
have to be catered to by consultants. Staff training and the
introduction of more effective systems of planning,
budgeting, financial control, maintenance and tax collection
are regarded as essential.
(k) Appraisal should not only establish a subproject's viability
but also, to the extent possible, given the Local Authority's
limited planning capabilities, look at sectoral policies and
ascertain whether the subproject contributes towards those
policies.
(1) In preparing and appraising specific sectoral components,
Local Authorities as well as the Borrower shall be guided by
the more detailed criteria set forth for the various sectors
below.
(m)  Through the Project, Local Authorities will be assisted to
improve housing and infrastructure investment programming,
and subprojects should be in conformity with these investment
programs. Local Authorities that do not have an overall
housing and infrastructure investment program should be
required to prepare a medium-term program as follows: Where
an up to date relevant master plan for a Local Authority does
not exist, an Integrated Infrastructure Plan should be
prepared (Terms of Reference have previously been suggested)
from which capital investments can more easily be identified,
planned, and programmed in the Housing, Primary
Infrastructure, and Maintenance Investment Progr&m to be
prepared.



Annex 18
- 163 -                        Page 8 of 21
Attachment
6.       SUBSECTOR OBJECTIVES AND SPECIFIC GUIDELINES FOR PROJECT SELECTION
AND APPRAISAL
(A)      WATER SUPPLY
(a) Objectives in the water subsector are:
(i)   maximising the productivity of existing investments by
rehabilitating plant and equipment where it is economic to do
so and by establishing a cost effective program of
procurement, inventory control and maintenance;
(ii)   improving service delivery through modifications to existing
plant and networks which will increase main pressures and
reduce system losses;
(iii)   improving service delivery to unserved populations and areas
of important economic activity through minor network
extension;
(iv)   improving plant and network capacity through rehabilitation
and new investments, to cope with increasing demand; and
(v)   strengthening institutional capabilities to plan, extend,
maintain, manage and finance - particularly with respect to
improved rates, billing and collection procedures - plant,
networks and services through appropriate technical assistance
and training.
(b) Project Selection and Appraisal Criteria for Water Supply Projects
are:
(i)   the extent of effective demand from domestic, commercial,
industrial and institutional populations as evidenced by
household income, expenditure and affordability surveys;
(ii)   the potential for yielding significant efficiency gains and
service delivery improvements through rehabilitating existing
plant and equipment, providing essential spares, reducing
leakage and/or adopting improved management and maintenance
methods; and
(iii)   the potential for enhancing net revenues of water supply
investments through improving service reliability and billing
collection.
(c) Technical Design and Evaluation Checklist
It is unrealistic to establish rigid technical design standards
that can be applied to a:l water supply projects in all Local Authorities.
However, at a minimum, the following data should be collected and assessed,
and should form the basis for standards which are appropriate to the level
of development of the Local Authority and the national standards and
practices for water supply:



- 164 -                        Annex 18
Page 9 of 21
Attachment
(i)   percentage of the urban population being served at the
following service levels: no service, tube well with handpump
(untreated), public standpipe, yard connection, or house
connections;
(ii)   percentage of the population at each service level
experiencing major service delivery problems due to : source
constraints, equipment failure, shortage of spares, shortages
of chenicals for treatment, system leakages, system design
constraints;
(iii)   per capita domestic water consumption at various service
levels;
(iv)   institutional (schools, hospitals, government buildings, etc.)
and industrial consumption as a percentage of total domestic
consumption;
(v)   peak day factor;
(vi)   peak hour factor;
(vii)   minimum and maximum flow velocities in pipes;
(viii)   minimum and maxim:n supply pressures;
(ix)   minimum size (diameter) of distribution mains;
(x)   minimum standards for pipe materials;
(xi)  minimum depth of cover over pipes;
(xii)   minimum water quality criteria (bacteria count, turbidity,
color, residual chlorine, etc).
(xiii)   minimum level of treatment;
(xiv)   public standpipes - number of inhabitants served per standpipe
per hydrant, minimum pipe size if hydrant on system, minimum
pressure and flow for fire fighting purposes.
(B)          ROADS
(a) Objectives in the urban roads subsector are:
(i)   maximising the productivity of existing investments, and
improving transportation sp'-vices for the urban population by
rehabilitation of existing heavily trafficked routes where it
is economic to do so;
(ii)   improving transportation services to important areas of
productive" economic activity (e.g. CBD's, markets, industrial
areas, etc.) by rehabilitating important existing roads;



- 165 -                       Annex I8
Page 10 of 21
Attachment
(iii)   relieving congestion by rehabilitating existing heavily
trafficked routes or by developing/rehabilitating by-pass
roads;
(iv)   facilitating orderly urban growth by developinglimproving
strategic sections of the urban road network; and
(v)   strengthening institutional capacity to plan efficiently,
manage finance, maintain and extend existing networks and
services, and to cope with increasing demand through
appropriate technical assistance and training.
(b) Projects Selection and Appraisal Criteria for Urban Road Projects
are:
ti)   potential for coping with present demand and meeting future
demand as established by traffic surveys and projections;
(ii)   potential for improving access to important areas of
productive economic activity (e.g. CBD's markets, industrial
areas, etc.) and to areas housing a high percentage of the
urban population;
(iii)   potential for improving vehicular flow and reducing
bottlenecks in the road network; and
(iv)   potential for reducing vehicle operating cost.
(c) Technical Design and Evaluation Checklistt
Road design standards will vary considerably both within and
between Local Authorities. However, the following minimum data must be
collected and evaluated, and should form the basis for road designs which
are of an appropriate standard considering the relative level of
development of the Local Authority 0-e volume of traffic to be
accommodated and the national stane  is and practices.
(i)   density of paved and unpaved road network per unit area
(meters per hectare) or per unit number of inhabitants
(kilcmeters per 10,000 inhabitants);
(ii)   minimum and maximum design speeds for arterials, secondary and
tertiary roads;
(iii)   minimum and maximum curve radius for each category of road;
(iv)   maximum slopes for each category of road;
(v)   minimum and maximum widths of roads, individual lanes, paved
areas for each category of road;
(vi)   minimum widths of sidewalks (paved and uipaved) for each
cateaorv of road;



- 166 -                        Annex 18
Page 11 of 21
Attachment
(vii)   minimum and maximum design loads for roads, bridges, culverts
and precast drain covers;
(viii)   maximum future, average and peak traffic volumes:
(ix)   standard road capacities for roads of different available
widths, numbers of lanes and average traffic speeds; and
(x)   details of road structure, such as sub-base, base course and
wearing course (materials and thickness).
(C)          DRAINAGE
(a) Objectives of the drainage subsector are:
(i)   relieving the incidence of flood damage to roads, property and
to economic activity, relieving road congestions due to
flooding, and improving environmental condition by
rehabilitating/developing/improving existing primary,
secondary and tertiary systems; and
(ii)   strengthening institutional capacity to efficiently plan,
manage, finance, maintain and extend existing systems and
networks through appropriate technical assistance and
training.
(b) Project Selection & Appraisal Criteria for Drainage Projects are:
(i)   potential for preventing damage to roads, property and
economic activity;
(ii)   potential for relieving congestion due to flooding;
(iii)   potential for reducing the incidence of disease and other
public health hazards and nuisances;
(iv)   potential for increased property values as a result of
improved drainage; and
(v)   potential for increasing the availability of land for
residential, commercial or industrial usage.
(c) Technical Design & Evaluation Checklist:
The following data should be collected and assessed and form the
basis for appropriate standards, considering the level of development of
the Local Authority and the national standards and practice.
(i)   flood return period for the design of primary, secondary and
tertiary drains;
(ii)   rainfall intensity - duration - frequency curve for the
r14 t4e. nn -



- 167 -                       Annex 18
Page 12 of 21
Attachment
(iii)   calculation method for flood discharges (rational or modified
rational formula, time of concentration, storage coefficient).
(iv)   run-off coefficients for different surface categories (rural,
light, heavy built-up, paved areas, roads, etc);
(v)   minWium and maximum flow velocities in drains, considering
factors of self-cleansing and safety;
(vi)   roughness coefficient of drain surfaces;
(vii)   maximum depth of drains (considering need for fences and
barriers);
(viii)   minimum freeboard of drains;
(ix)   type of drains (concrete limit, precast sections, earthlined);
and
(x)   minimum size of closed conduits (culverts) for cleansing
access.
(D)          WASTEWATER COLLECTION AND DISPOSAL (SANITATION)
(a) Objectives of the wastewater collection and disposal of sanitation
subsector are:
(i)   improving domestic hygiene, reducing the incidence of
infectious disease, and reducing environmental pollution
through the construction/rehabilitation of sewerage and sewage
treatment systems or communal or individual toilet/disposal
facilities in residential areas, and in strategic public areas
such as markets, bus terminals; and
(ii)   strengthening institutional capacity to efficientlr plan,
manage, finance, maintain, and improve existing s)stems of
collection, treatment and disposal through appropriate
technical assistance and training.
(b) Project Selection & Appraisal Criteria for sanitation projects are:
(i)   extent of effective demand from domestic, commercial,
industrial and institutional populations as evidenced by
overcrowding of existing facilities and/or by household
income, expenditure and affordability surveys; and
(ii)   potential for improving domestic hygiene and reducing the
incidence of infectious disease.
(c) Technical Design and Evaluation Checklist:



Annex 18
-168 -                       Page 13 of 21
Attachment
Dependant on existing arrangements, residential densities, subsoil
conditions, estimated costs and standards and practices of those Local
Authorities responsible for public health and environment, sanitation
projects would normally involve the provision or expansion of piped
waterborne sewerage systems discharging to oxidation ponds or individual or
blocks of toilets discharging to septic tanks or other appropriate disposal
means.
(i)   daily per capita waste generation, including estimate of
percentage of potable water to waste and peak factors;
(ii)   percentage of population being served at different service
levels (i.e. to sewer, to septic tank, other);
(iii)   densities of development;
(iv)   topography and subsoil conditions;
(v)   institutional, industrial and commercial discharges;
(vi)   sewage strengths and composition;
(vii)   minimum and maximum flow velocities (gradients) of pipe;
(viii)   minimum size (diameter) of pipes;
(ix)   minimum standards of pipe materials;
(x)   minimum standards of pipe materials;
(xi)   maximum spacing of manholes (rodding eyes);
(xii)   house connection arrangements;
(xiii)   minimum level of treatment;
(xiv)   discharge point/usage of treated effluent for individual
systems;
(xv)   number of inhabitants per latrine;
(xvi)   area served by public latrine;
(xvii) maximum walking distance to public latrine;
(xviii)   minimum design standards (size, material, distances to houses,
pit volume and dimensions, ventilation, etc.) of public
latrines;
(xix)   optimum number of individual units per latrine block;
(xx)   frequency of latrine emptying;



- 169 -                       Annex 18
Page 14 of 21
Attachment
(xxi)   minimu  capacity of vacuum tankers required for emptying; and
(xxii)   minimum standards for septic tanks (chamber volumes, tile
field lengths, soakway pit size, soil percolation rates, etc.)
(E)          SOLID WASTE MANAGEMENT
(a) Objectives of the solid waste subsector are:
(i)   maximizing the productivity of existing investments by
rehabilitating existing plant, collection vehicles, and other
equipment where it is econLomic to do so, and by establishing
cost effective systems of collection and disposal, maintenance
programming, inventory control, and procurement;
(ii)   improving collection services by more efficient planning of
collection routes, pick-up times, and allocation of collection
vehicles and equipment;
(iii)   reducing environmental pollution and drain maintenance
problems and improving public health by adopting more
efficient methods of collection and disposal;
(iv)   extending collection services to cover important unse-ved
residential areas, and to cover important unserved areas of
productive economic activity;
(v)   extending/improving collection services to cope with
increasing demand; and
(vi)   strengthening institutional capacity to efficiently plan,
manage, finance, maintain, and extend existing services to
cope with increasing demand through appropriate technical
assistance and training, and through improved billing and
collection of fees.
(b) Project Selection and Appraisal Criteria for Solid Waste Management
Projects are:
Ci)   extent of effective demand from domestic, commercial,
industrial and institutional populations by household income,
expenditure and affordability surveys;
(ii)   potential for improving domestic hygiene and reducing the
incidence of infectious disease;
(iii)   potential for reducing the incidence of disease and other
public health hazards and nuisances; and
(iv)   potential for private sector involvement in refuse collection.
(c) Technical Design and Evaluation Checklist:



Annex 18
- 170 -                       Page 15 of 21
Attachment
The following data should be collected and assessed and form the
basis for design standards which are appropriated to the level of
development of the Local Authority and, the standards and practices of
authorities responsible for solid waste man&gement.
(i)   per ca,'ita volume of refuse;
(ii)   unit weight of refuse;
(iii)   unit composition of refuse;
(iv)   compactability of refuse;
(v)   maximum capacity of refuse collection points (maximum walking
distance);
(vi)   number of inhabitants served per collection point;
Cvii)   volume of refuse generated per day;
(viii)   frequency of collection;
(ix)   number and capacity of operational collection vehicles;
'x)   number and capacity of collection vehicles potentially
available for service (i.e. fleet size and capacity,
distinguishing potentially operational from those beyond
repair);
(xi)   landfill site capacity, and minimum and maximum lifetime;
(xii)   minimum environmental standards for landfill operation
(groundwater protection, air pollution, etc.)
(F)          STREETLIGHTING
(a) Objectives of the streetlighting subsectors are:
(i)   maximising the productivity of existing investments by
rehabilitating existing poles, lights and service cables where
it is economic to do so, and by establishing a cost effective
program of on-going maintenance, inventory control, and
procurement;
(ii)   improving urban safety, productivity, and convenience by
extending street lighting to cover important urban population
areas, and important unserved areas of productive economic
activity; and
(iii)   strengthening institutional capacity to efficiently plan,
manage, finance, maintain and extend existing networks, and to
cope with increasing demand through appropriate technical
assistance and training.



-171 -                       Annex 18
Page 16 of 21
Attachment
(b) Project Selection and Appraisal Criteria for Streetlighting
Projects are:
ti)   extent of effective demand from domestic, commercial,
industrial and institutional populations as evidenced by
surveys indicating a willingness and capacity to pay for
extended or improved service.
(ii)   potential for benefitting a significant percentage of the
urban poor;
(iii)   potential for yielding:  ERRs significantly in excess of the
cost of funds; high benefit cost ratios; or effectiveness cost
ratios; and
(iv)   feasibility of implementation (planning, design, contract
documentation, construction, supervision and maintenance) by
the public and private sectors.
(c) Technical Design and Evaluation Checklist:
The following data must be collected and assessed and form the
basis for design standards which are appropriate to the level of
development of the Local Authority and the standards and practices of
authorities responsible fcr street lighting.
(i)    location/spacing standards (i.e. whether on either or both
sides of road and spacing of poles);
(ii)   class/type and condition of road;
(iii)   current measures for streetlighting (types of poles, lanterns,
etc.); and
(iv)   existing capacity for power supply at the local level by ZESA.
!b          7.           OBJECTIVES AND GUIDELINES FOR FINANCIAL APPRAISAL OF PROJECTS
(A)          OBJECTIVES
(a)   sufficient funds are available on a timely basis to meet the
costs specified from the technical appraisal;
(b)   that the proposed level of spending produces an acceptable
rate or return;
(c)   that the resulting sale price, fees or charges are affordable
to the intended beneficiaries; and
(d)   that the required financial returns are achievable given
changes in assumptions on critical variables, namely costs and
timing.



- 172 -                        Annex 18
Page 17 of 21
Attachment
(B)          GUIDELINES
(a) Cost Identification
(i)   The costs to be included in each project will consist of
*   cost of land acquisition.  Lard will be either purchased or
transferred to the project at market price or at a value
determined by the Borrower. Costs will include legal and
survey fees and associated administrative expenses
* cost of infrastructure including off site costs, site
reticulation costs and land reclamatiGn
* on plot development costs
* cost of planning, design and supervision of contracts
* operational costs including interest during construction
* contingencies for physical and price changes
(ii)   Costs will be classified into capital and revenue.  Capital
costs are those costs incurred for the construction of an
asset whereas revenue costs are incurred:
*   to pay interest on borrowings; and
* to operate and maintain the asset after construction.
(b) Affordability
The revenue costs of the investment program will be met by the
Local Authority from its annual revenue budget. Unless the project is able
to become self financing immediately, an additional cost burden will be
imposed on the Local Authority. These additional costs will have to be
funded from the sale proceeds, or increases in taxes, fees and charges.
The financial appraisal must include a preliminary assessment of
how much the beneficiary, consumer or community is able to afford over and
above their present financial commitment to the Local Authority. In making
this assessment, judgements will have to be made on how the project costs
(both capital and revenue) are to be allocated and what mechanisms will be
adopted to achieve cost recovery. Project benefits accrue to different
groups of beneficiaries from whom it is legitimate to require payment to



- 173 -                         Annex 18
Page 15 of 21
Attachment
recover costs. A simple general rule is that project costs which are
specific to a beneficiary should be al ocated to and recovered from the
direct beneficiary. Project costs which result in benefits shared by the
wider community should be recovered from them usually in the form of the
prevailing taxes, fees or charges.
The charging base will vary according to the type of service being
provided, the policy of the Borrower and the preference of local
Authorities. However, irrespective of the cost recovery option adopted,
the financial appraisal must consider the affordable level of payments by
the target group of beneficiaries. It will be necessary to calculate the
available household income either from published statistical data or by
carrying out a household survey. It is normal practice for government to
stipulate the maximum per_entage of disposable household income which is
"allowable- to meet Local Authority charges. For example the norm for
housing rents and service charges is 25Z of income.
If the assessment of affordability indicates that the target group
Falls outside the 'allowable" norm then the options are to redesign the
project and lower the cost or look to alternative sources for possible
subsidies or funding.
(c) Cash Flow Analysis
The impact of the project costs on the Local Authorities' funding
requirements will need to be assessed through the production of a detailed
Cash Flow Statement.  This Statement will detail the inflow and outflow of
cash for each year over the expected construction period and life of the
asset. The order of items to be classified should be as follows:-
(i) Sources:
loans
grants
reserves
income from taxes, fees and charges
(ii) Application/Usess
land acquisition and reclamation
construction costs
design and supervision costs
administration and overhead costs
loan repayments
working capital
It is probable that the analysis will reveal a significant funding
gap unless a Local Authority's tariffs and charges are raised specifically
to provide the necessary funding. At this stage the options for price
increases will need to be quantified and preferred options identified for
inclusion in the financial appraisal. The implementation of these proposed
tariff changes should be regarded as an essential prerequisite of project
approval.



- 174 -                       Annex 18
Page 19 of 21
Attachment
(d) Impact on the Local Authority's Revenue Budget
Most of the Local Authority capital investment will not in itself
generate sufficient additional income to meet the recurrent revenue cost of
financing loan charges and operating the asset. Therefore, the additional
income will have to be derived from alternative existing sources of funds
unless the council decides, by default, to permit funding deficits to be
incurred.  In terms of prudent financial management, all additional costs
should be funded from increased revenues derived f-om the imposition of
higher taxes, fees or charges. Specifically this will involve the Local
Authority in estimating the cost of the following:-                                          t
(i) the loan charges; and
(ii) additional staffing and running costs.
These additional costs may be reduced by any incremental revenue
generated by the project, as for example, the increased sales of water
supplied as a result of water supply project. The final selection of an
increase to apply will rest with the discretion af the Local Authority.
Affecting the selection will include such factors as the possible yield
from the tariff or tax change, administrative and legal complexity, the
requirement for the Borrower's approval and the willingness of the
community to pay.
Ce) Internal Rate of Return
For those projects which generate surpluses (or positive cash
flows) it is desirable to calculate the internal rate of return. These
projects will tend to be of a commercial or income generating nature rather
than of a social/welfare nature. The internal rate of return should equal
or exceed the current cost of capital to the Local Authority.
(f) Sensitivity Analysis
The projected cash flow statement and internal rate of return
calculation is based on a series of assumptions ranging from the phasing of
construction costs to the realisation of funds. Such assumptions are
naturally based or. the best information available at the time of the
analyses.
Some of the assumptions made may be critical to the long term
viability of the project. Therefore, the effect of possible changes in
those assumptions should be tested and their impact on the cash flow
demonstrated.
8.       OBJECTIVES AND GUIDELINES FOR THE INSTITUTIONAL APPRAISAL OF
PROJECTS
(A)      OBJECTIVES
In addition to completing technical and financial appraisals of
projects, it is necessary to assess the capability of a Local Authority to
implenant the project and to manage the asset after its construction.
Specifically, the two factors to be assessed are as followst-



-175 -                         Annex 18
Page 20 of 21
Attachment
(a) the quantity and quality of managerial and technical skills;
and
(b) the adequacy of the systems and procedures for the
utilisation and maintenance of the asset.
The assessment will be of a qualitative nature and will depend
upon the availability of sufficiently skilled and experienced evaluators.
To a large extent the qualitative assessment will be based upon the Local
Authority's previous success or failure in managing proiects of a similar
nature and upon the availability of consultancy assistance.
Although this assessment may not be capable of such precise
definition as the technical and financial appraisals, it is essential that
there are the r.cessary management skills in place to ensure that the
maximum benefit 7U derived from the investment.  The investment will be
justified financ.ally on the assumption of a specified operating life.  If,
due to inadequate maintenance procedures, or insufficient supervision, the
equipment breaks down, then the financial viability is undermined.
(B)      ASSESSMENT OF MANAGERIAL AND TECHNICAL CAPABILITIES
The technical skills required to implement the project will be
readily identifiable. The skills will be available in house or provided by
consultants. The assessment of managerial capabilities is more
problematical. One reliable indicator is the previous record of the Local
Authority in successfully implementing and maintaining similar projects.
Any proposal for large investments involving new technical skills should be
closely appraised.
For major infrastructure projects such as water treatment plants,
sewage treatment plants, pipeliaes, major roads and bridges, the need for a
professionally qualified and suitably experienced engineer (10 year
experience since qualification in the appropriate or related sector) to be
responRible for the construction of the investment would be a minimum
requirement. Depending on the size of the proposed investment, it would
also be necessary for the superintending engineer to have adequate support
staff (e.g. a graduate engineer and experienced works supervisor as a
minimum). For investments in excess of about Z$5 million, two qualified
engineers would need to be involved in the implementation of the
investment. If such skills are not available within the implementing
agency for full time involvement on the project, the agency would need to
demonstrate how such skills would be acquired and paid for (e.g. engagement
of consultants). Where specialist works (e.g. structural, electrical and
mechanical engineering) are necessary, the appraisal would include a review
of how such skills may to be provided. Qualified and experienced engineers
in the respective disciplines would again be required to take
responsilbility for implementation of major projects (e.g. >Z$1.5 million).
Where the Local Authority proposes to carry out a project by
direct labour, the qualification, experience, skill levels and numbers of
Local Authority personnel would be reviewed, as well as available plant,
equipment, vehicles and systems (see below) to insure that they are
adequate to carry out the works to the required values in the time



- 176 -                        Annex 18
Page 21 of 21
Attachment
For projects which would add major assets to the Local Authority's
inventory (e.g. treatment plants), the appraisal would review proposals for
staffing, equipment and annual recurrent budget allocation - not to be less
than 12 per annum of capital value to ensure such assets can, in the
future, be adequately maintained.
(C)      ASSESSMENT OF SYSTEMS AND PROCEDURES
It is important that the managerial and technical skills be
founded upon well-established and documented syetems and procedures within
the Local Authority. There should be clear prozedures and arrangements for
the planning. control and reporting of activities.
The tendering procedures for contracts must be sufficiently
detailed to provide for a secure and impartial selection of contractors.
These procedures should be set out in the Local Authority's published
regulations.
It is essential that there are project costing systems in use by
the Local Authority's under the supervision of an experienced finance
manager/accountant. For contracts greater than Z35.0 million, a full time
bookkeeper/cost clerk should be employed. Accounting records need to be
maintained on an up to date basis so that significant variations in project
costs are reported to senior management for corrective action to be taken.
Project costs should be reported on a monthly basis. Also it is desirable
that there be an independent check of contract payments, preferably by an
auditor, prior to the payment being made. All contracts greater than Z$l.O
million should be subject to an audit and all claims under a contract
should be scrutinised independently from the supervising engineer.
For example, in the maintenance of plant and equipment, there
should be satisfactory procedures for determining servicing intervals and
standards for defining, supervising and recording the work carried out, for
reporting defects and for monitoring costs.
In carrying out an appraisal, there should be sufficient
confidence in the existing systems for the effective control and use of the
proposed investment. If weaknesses are identified at the appraisal stage,
then consultancy assistance or advice should be sought by the Local
Authority to improve its ability to derive the intended long term benefits
from the investment.
